#### Applications of Mid-band Range



The mid-band range of a linear amplifier is crucial in various applications, including wireless communication, optical wireless communication, and satellite communication. In wireless communication, the mid-band range is used to amplify the signals in the intermediate frequency (IF) range, which is typically between 10 MHz to 300 MHz. This range is important as it allows for efficient amplification of the signals without distortion, resulting in better signal quality.



In optical wireless communication, the mid-band range is used to amplify the signals in the optical frequency range, which is typically between 300 GHz to 3 THz. This range is important as it allows for efficient amplification of the signals without distortion, resulting in better signal quality. Additionally, variations of optical wireless communication can be employed in a diverse range of communication applications, ranging from optical interconnects within integrated circuits to outdoor inter-building links and satellite communications.



In satellite communication, the mid-band range is used to amplify the signals in the microwave frequency range, which is typically between 1 GHz to 100 GHz. This range is important as it allows for efficient amplification of the signals without distortion, resulting in better signal quality. Furthermore, the mid-band range is crucial in satellite communication as it allows for the transmission of high-frequency signals over long distances.



#### Factors Affecting Mid-band Range in Linear Amplifiers



The mid-band range of a linear amplifier is affected by various factors, including the type of amplifying device used, the circuit design, and the frequency response of the current mirrors used in the amplifier. These factors can impact the linearity and frequency response of the amplifier, ultimately affecting its mid-band range. For example, the type of amplifying device used can greatly impact the mid-band range of a linear amplifier. Different types of amplifying devices have different frequency responses, which can affect the linearity of the amplifier's output. Additionally, the circuit design plays a crucial role in determining the mid-band range of a linear amplifier. The design of the circuit can affect the frequency response of the amplifier, which in turn affects its mid-band range.



In conclusion, the mid-band range is a crucial aspect of linear amplifiers and has various applications in wireless communication, optical wireless communication, and satellite communication. The type of amplifying device used and the circuit design are important factors that can affect the mid-band range of a linear amplifier. As technology continues to advance, it is essential to consider the mid-band range in the design and development of linear amplifiers to ensure efficient and high-quality signal amplification.





## Chapter: - Chapter 6: Linear Amplifiers:



### Section: - Section: 6.5 Differential Amplifiers:



### Subsection (optional): 6.5a Introduction to Differential Amplifiers



Differential amplifiers are an essential component in the design of linear amplifiers. They are used to amplify the difference between two input signals, while rejecting any common-mode signals that are present. This makes them ideal for applications where the desired signal is small compared to the noise or interference present in the system.



Differential amplifiers are commonly used in audio amplifiers, instrumentation amplifiers, and operational amplifiers. They are also used in communication systems, such as wireless communication, optical wireless communication, and satellite communication, to amplify the signals in the intermediate frequency, optical frequency, and microwave frequency ranges, respectively.



## Design challenges



### Input Impedance



One of the main design challenges in differential amplifiers is maintaining a high input impedance. This is important because a high input impedance ensures that the amplifier does not load the input signals, which can affect the accuracy and linearity of the amplifier. To achieve a high input impedance, the differential amplifier is typically designed using a cascode configuration, where the input transistors are connected in series with a common-source amplifier. This configuration allows for a high input impedance while maintaining a low output impedance.



### Common-Mode Rejection Ratio (CMRR)



Another important design challenge in differential amplifiers is achieving a high common-mode rejection ratio (CMRR). CMRR is a measure of how well the amplifier rejects any common-mode signals that are present at the input. A high CMRR is crucial in applications where the desired signal is small compared to the common-mode signal, as it ensures that the amplifier amplifies only the desired signal and rejects any unwanted noise or interference.



To achieve a high CMRR, the differential amplifier is designed with a balanced input stage, where the two input transistors are matched in terms of their physical characteristics and operating conditions. This ensures that any common-mode signals present at the input are equally amplified and canceled out at the output.



### Frequency Response



The frequency response of a differential amplifier is another important design consideration. It is crucial to ensure that the amplifier has a flat frequency response in the mid-band range, where it is typically used. This is achieved by selecting appropriate biasing and compensation techniques, as well as using high-quality components with low parasitic effects.



## Applications of Differential Amplifiers



Differential amplifiers have a wide range of applications in various fields, including audio amplifiers, instrumentation amplifiers, and operational amplifiers. In audio amplifiers, differential amplifiers are used to amplify the difference between the left and right audio channels, resulting in a stereo output. In instrumentation amplifiers, they are used to amplify small signals from sensors or transducers, while rejecting any common-mode noise or interference. In operational amplifiers, differential amplifiers are used as the input stage, providing high gain and high input impedance.



In communication systems, differential amplifiers are used to amplify the signals in the intermediate frequency, optical frequency, and microwave frequency ranges, as mentioned earlier. In wireless communication, they are used in the receiver circuit to amplify the signals in the intermediate frequency range, while rejecting any common-mode noise or interference. In optical wireless communication, they are used in the transceiver circuit to amplify the signals in the optical frequency range, while in satellite communication, they are used in the transmitter and receiver circuits to amplify the signals in the microwave frequency range.



## Conclusion



Differential amplifiers are an essential component in the design of linear amplifiers, providing high gain, high input impedance, and high CMRR. They have a wide range of applications in various fields, including audio amplifiers, instrumentation amplifiers, and operational amplifiers, as well as in communication systems. To overcome the design challenges in differential amplifiers, careful selection of components and appropriate biasing and compensation techniques are crucial. 





## Chapter: - Chapter 6: Linear Amplifiers:



### Section: - Section: 6.5 Differential Amplifiers:



### Subsection (optional): 6.5b Differential Amplifiers in Linear Amplifiers



Differential amplifiers play a crucial role in the design of linear amplifiers. They are used to amplify the difference between two input signals, while rejecting any common-mode signals that may be present. This makes them ideal for applications where the desired signal is small compared to the noise or interference present in the system.



Differential amplifiers are commonly used in a variety of electronic systems, including audio amplifiers, instrumentation amplifiers, and operational amplifiers. They are also used in communication systems, such as wireless communication, optical wireless communication, and satellite communication, to amplify signals in the intermediate frequency, optical frequency, and microwave frequency ranges, respectively.



## Design challenges



### Input Impedance



One of the main design challenges in differential amplifiers is maintaining a high input impedance. This is important because a high input impedance ensures that the amplifier does not load the input signals, which can affect the accuracy and linearity of the amplifier. To achieve a high input impedance, the differential amplifier is typically designed using a cascode configuration, where the input transistors are connected in series with a common-source amplifier. This configuration allows for a high input impedance while maintaining a low output impedance.



### Common-Mode Rejection Ratio (CMRR)



Another important design challenge in differential amplifiers is achieving a high common-mode rejection ratio (CMRR). CMRR is a measure of how well the amplifier rejects any common-mode signals that are present at the input. A high CMRR is crucial in applications where the desired signal is small compared to the common-mode signal, as it ensures that the amplifier amplifies only the desired signal and rejects any unwanted noise or interference.



To address these design challenges, differential amplifiers are often designed using operational transconductance amplifiers (OTAs). These devices have a high input impedance and a high CMRR, making them well-suited for use in differential amplifiers.



## Differential Amplifiers in Linear Amplifiers



Differential amplifiers are an essential component in the design of linear amplifiers. They are typically used in the input stage of the amplifier to amplify the small input signal and provide a high gain. This allows for a larger output signal, which can then be further amplified by subsequent stages in the amplifier.



In linear amplifiers, differential amplifiers are often used in conjunction with other components, such as resistors and capacitors, to shape the frequency response and suppress voltage transients. This ensures that the amplifier operates within its desired frequency range and produces a clean output signal.



## Subsequent improvements



As with any technology, differential amplifiers have undergone improvements and advancements over time. One notable improvement is the addition of diodes and an I<sub>bias</sub> terminal in later versions of the OTA. These additions have two main benefits: they increase the linearity of the input stage and offset much of the temperature sensitivity of the OTA's transconductance.



Another improvement is the integration of an optional-use output buffer amplifier on the same chip as the OTA. This allows for a more compact and convenient design, as well as the option to use the OTA as a traditional op-amp by converting its output current to a voltage.



An example of a chip that incorporates both of these improvements is the National Semiconductor LM13600 and its successor, the L





## Chapter: - Chapter 6: Linear Amplifiers:



### Section: - Section: 6.5 Differential Amplifiers:



### Subsection (optional): 6.5c Applications of Differential Amplifiers



Differential amplifiers are an essential component in the design of linear amplifiers. They are used to amplify the difference between two input signals, while rejecting any common-mode signals that may be present. This makes them ideal for applications where the desired signal is small compared to the noise or interference present in the system.



In this section, we will explore some of the common applications of differential amplifiers in various electronic systems.



#### Audio Amplifiers



One of the most common applications of differential amplifiers is in audio amplifiers. In audio systems, the desired signal is often small compared to the noise and interference present in the system. Differential amplifiers are used to amplify the difference between the left and right audio channels, while rejecting any common-mode signals that may be present. This results in a cleaner and more accurate audio signal.



#### Instrumentation Amplifiers



Instrumentation amplifiers are used to amplify small signals from sensors and transducers. These signals are often susceptible to noise and interference, which can affect the accuracy of the measurement. Differential amplifiers are used in instrumentation amplifiers to amplify the difference between the signal and the common-mode noise, resulting in a more accurate measurement.



#### Operational Amplifiers



Operational amplifiers, or op-amps, are widely used in electronic circuits for various applications such as amplification, filtering, and signal conditioning. Differential amplifiers are a key component in the design of op-amps, as they provide high gain and high input impedance, making them ideal for amplifying small signals.



#### Communication Systems



Differential amplifiers are also used in communication systems, such as wireless communication, optical wireless communication, and satellite communication. In these systems, differential amplifiers are used to amplify signals in the intermediate frequency, optical frequency, and microwave frequency ranges, respectively. They are also used to reject any common-mode signals that may be present, resulting in a cleaner and more accurate signal.



## Design Challenges



### Input Impedance



One of the main design challenges in differential amplifiers is maintaining a high input impedance. This is important because a high input impedance ensures that the amplifier does not load the input signals, which can affect the accuracy and linearity of the amplifier. To achieve a high input impedance, the differential amplifier is typically designed using a cascode configuration, where the input transistors are connected in series with a common-source amplifier. This configuration allows for a high input impedance while maintaining a low output impedance.



### Common-Mode Rejection Ratio (CMRR)



Another important design challenge in differential amplifiers is achieving a high common-mode rejection ratio (CMRR). CMRR is a measure of how well the amplifier rejects any common-mode signals that are present at the input. A high CMRR is crucial in applications where the desired signal is small compared to the common-mode signal, as it ensures that the amplifier amplifies only the desired signal and rejects any unwanted noise or interference.



In conclusion, differential amplifiers have a wide range of applications in various electronic systems, making them an essential component in the design of linear amplifiers. Their ability to amplify small signals while rejecting common-mode signals makes them a valuable tool in achieving accurate and reliable signal amplification. 





## Chapter: - Chapter 6: Linear Amplifiers:



### Section: - Section: 6.6 Cascode Amplifiers:



### Subsection (optional): 6.6a Introduction to Cascode Amplifiers



Cascode amplifiers are a type of multistage amplifier that combines the advantages of both common-emitter and common-base configurations. They are commonly used in high-frequency applications due to their high gain and bandwidth, as well as their ability to provide a high input impedance and low output impedance.



#### Basic Cascode Amplifier Configuration



The basic cascode amplifier configuration consists of two transistors, one connected in common-emitter configuration and the other in common-base configuration. The common-emitter transistor provides high gain, while the common-base transistor provides a high input impedance. The output of the common-emitter transistor is connected to the input of the common-base transistor, creating a cascaded effect.



#### Advantages of Cascode Amplifiers



Cascode amplifiers offer several advantages over other amplifier configurations. These include:



- High gain: The cascaded effect of the two transistors results in a higher overall gain compared to a single-stage amplifier.

- High input impedance: The common-base transistor provides a high input impedance, which reduces the loading effect on the input signal.

- Low output impedance: The common-emitter transistor provides a low output impedance, which allows for better matching with the load.

- Wide bandwidth: The high gain and low output impedance of cascode amplifiers make them suitable for high-frequency applications.

- Improved linearity: The cascaded effect of the two transistors results in improved linearity, making cascode amplifiers suitable for applications that require accurate amplification of small signals.



#### Applications of Cascode Amplifiers



Cascode amplifiers find applications in various electronic systems, including:



- High-frequency amplifiers: The high gain and wide bandwidth of cascode amplifiers make them suitable for use in high-frequency amplifiers, such as those used in radio frequency (RF) communication systems.

- Audio amplifiers: Cascode amplifiers can be used in audio amplifiers to provide high gain and low distortion, resulting in a cleaner and more accurate audio signal.

- Instrumentation amplifiers: The high input impedance and low output impedance of cascode amplifiers make them suitable for use in instrumentation amplifiers, which are used to amplify small signals from sensors and transducers.

- Operational amplifiers: Cascode amplifiers are a key component in the design of operational amplifiers, providing high gain and high input impedance.

- Differential amplifiers: Cascode amplifiers can be used in differential amplifiers to amplify the difference between two input signals while rejecting any common-mode signals that may be present.



In the next section, we will explore the design and analysis of cascode amplifiers in more detail. 





# Title: Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide":



## Chapter: - Chapter 6: Linear Amplifiers:



### Section: - Section: 6.6 Cascode Amplifiers:



### Subsection (optional): 6.6b Cascode Amplifiers in Linear Amplifiers



Cascode amplifiers are a type of multistage amplifier that combines the advantages of both common-emitter and common-base configurations. They are commonly used in high-frequency applications due to their high gain and bandwidth, as well as their ability to provide a high input impedance and low output impedance.



#### Basic Cascode Amplifier Configuration



The basic cascode amplifier configuration consists of two transistors, one connected in common-emitter configuration and the other in common-base configuration. The common-emitter transistor provides high gain, while the common-base transistor provides a high input impedance. The output of the common-emitter transistor is connected to the input of the common-base transistor, creating a cascaded effect.



The cascode amplifier can be represented by the following circuit diagram:



$$

\begin{align}

V_{in} &\rightarrow &T_1 &\rightarrow &T_2 &\rightarrow V_{out} \\

& &\uparrow & &\uparrow \\

& &I_{C1} & &I_{C2}

\end{align}

$$



Where $V_{in}$ is the input voltage, $V_{out}$ is the output voltage, $T_1$ is the common-emitter transistor, $T_2$ is the common-base transistor, $I_{C1}$ is the collector current of $T_1$, and $I_{C2}$ is the collector current of $T_2$.



#### Advantages of Cascode Amplifiers



Cascode amplifiers offer several advantages over other amplifier configurations. These include:



- High gain: The cascaded effect of the two transistors results in a higher overall gain compared to a single-stage amplifier. The gain of the cascode amplifier can be calculated as:



$$

A_{v} = A_{v1} \times A_{v2}

$$



Where $A_{v1}$ is the voltage gain of $T_1$ and $A_{v2}$ is the voltage gain of $T_2$.



- High input impedance: The common-base transistor provides a high input impedance, which reduces the loading effect on the input signal. The input impedance of the cascode amplifier can be calculated as:



$$

Z_{in} = \frac{r_{\pi 1} + \beta_1 r_{o1}}{1 + \beta_1}

$$



Where $r_{\pi 1}$ is the input resistance of $T_1$, $\beta_1$ is the current gain of $T_1$, and $r_{o1}$ is the output resistance of $T_1$.



- Low output impedance: The common-emitter transistor provides a low output impedance, which allows for better matching with the load. The output impedance of the cascode amplifier can be calculated as:



$$

Z_{out} = \frac{r_{o2}}{1 + \beta_2}

$$



Where $r_{o2}$ is the output resistance of $T_2$ and $\beta_2$ is the current gain of $T_2$.



- Wide bandwidth: The high gain and low output impedance of cascode amplifiers make them suitable for high-frequency applications. The bandwidth of the cascode amplifier can be calculated as:



$$

BW = \frac{f_T}{A_{v1} \times A_{v2}}

$$



Where $f_T$ is the transition frequency of the transistors.



- Improved linearity: The cascaded effect of the two transistors results in improved linearity, making cascode amplifiers suitable for applications that require accurate amplification of small signals.



#### Applications of Cascode Amplifiers



Cascode amplifiers find applications in various electronic systems, including:



- High-frequency amplifiers: The high gain and wide bandwidth of cascode amplifiers make them suitable for use in high-frequency amplifiers, such as in radio frequency (RF) circuits.

- Audio amplifiers: The improved linearity of cascode amplifiers makes them suitable for use in audio amplifiers, where accurate amplification of small signals is important.

- Voltage regulators: The high input impedance and low output impedance of cascode amplifiers make them suitable for use in voltage regulators, where they can provide stable and accurate output voltages.

- Oscillators: The high gain and wide bandwidth of cascode amplifiers make them suitable for use in oscillators, where they can provide stable and high-frequency oscillations.



### Conclusion



In conclusion, cascode amplifiers are a versatile and useful type of multistage amplifier that offers high gain, high input impedance, low output impedance, wide bandwidth, and improved linearity. They find applications in various electronic systems and are particularly useful in high-frequency applications.

## Chapter: - Chapter 6: Linear Amplifiers:



### Section: - Section: 6.6 Cascode Amplifiers:



### Subsection (optional): 6.6c Applications of Cascode Amplifiers



Cascode amplifiers have a wide range of applications in the field of microelectronics. They are commonly used in high-frequency circuits, such as radio frequency (RF) amplifiers, due to their high gain and bandwidth. In this section, we will explore some of the common applications of cascode amplifiers.



#### RF Amplifiers



One of the most common applications of cascode amplifiers is in RF amplifiers. The high gain and bandwidth of cascode amplifiers make them ideal for amplifying high-frequency signals. In RF amplifiers, the cascode configuration is often used to provide a high input impedance and low output impedance, which helps to minimize signal loss and distortion.



#### Audio Amplifiers



Cascode amplifiers are also used in audio amplifiers, particularly in high-fidelity systems. The high gain and low distortion of cascode amplifiers make them well-suited for amplifying audio signals without introducing unwanted noise or distortion. In audio amplifiers, the cascode configuration is often used to provide a high input impedance and low output impedance, which helps to minimize signal loss and distortion.



#### Voltage Regulators



Cascode amplifiers are also commonly used in voltage regulators. In this application, the cascode configuration is used to provide a high input impedance and low output impedance, which helps to regulate the output voltage. The high gain of cascode amplifiers also helps to maintain a stable output voltage, even when there are fluctuations in the input voltage.



#### Differential Amplifiers



Another common application of cascode amplifiers is in differential amplifiers. In this configuration, two cascode amplifiers are connected in parallel, with one amplifier amplifying the positive input signal and the other amplifying the negative input signal. This results in a differential output signal, which is useful in applications such as instrumentation amplifiers and operational amplifiers.



#### Low-Noise Amplifiers



Cascode amplifiers are also used in low-noise amplifiers, particularly in high-frequency applications. The high gain and low noise of cascode amplifiers make them well-suited for amplifying weak signals without introducing additional noise. In low-noise amplifiers, the cascode configuration is often used to provide a high input impedance and low output impedance, which helps to minimize signal loss and distortion.



In conclusion, cascode amplifiers have a wide range of applications in the field of microelectronics, particularly in high-frequency circuits. Their high gain, low noise, and high input and output impedance make them a versatile and useful component in many electronic systems.

The single-transistor building block stages are the basic building blocks of electronic circuits, and they are responsible for amplifying, switching, and processing signals in a variety of applications. These stages are typically composed of a single transistor, along with a few passive components such as resistors and capacitors. Despite their simplicity, they play a critical role in the overall performance of electronic systems.



Throughout this chapter, we will cover the different types of single-transistor building block stages, including common-source, common-drain, and common-gate configurations. We will also discuss their small-signal models, which are essential for analyzing and designing circuits. Additionally, we will explore the effects of various parameters, such as biasing and load resistance, on the performance of these stages.

## Chapter 7: Single-transistor Building Block Stages:



### Section: 7.1 Common-source:



The common-source configuration is one of the most commonly used single-transistor building block stages in microelectronic devices and circuits. It is a type of amplifier that uses a field-effect transistor (FET) as its active device. In this section, we will introduce the common-source configuration and discuss its characteristics and applications.



#### 7.1a Introduction to Common-source



The common-source configuration is a type of amplifier that uses a FET as its active device. It is called "common-source" because the source terminal of the FET is connected to the ground, which is a common reference point in electronic circuits. The input signal is applied to the gate terminal, and the output signal is taken from the drain terminal. The common-source configuration is shown in Figure 1.



$$

\Delta w = \eta \cdot x_j(n) \cdot y_j(n)

$$



Figure 1: Common-source configuration



The common-source configuration has a high input impedance, which means that it does not draw much current from the input signal source. This makes it suitable for use in applications where the input signal source has a high output impedance. Additionally, the common-source configuration has a low output impedance, which means that it can drive a load with a low impedance without significant signal loss.



The small-signal model of the common-source configuration is shown in Figure 2. It consists of a voltage-controlled current source, representing the FET, and a load resistor, representing the output impedance of the circuit. The small-signal model is used to analyze the behavior of the circuit and determine its gain, input and output impedances, and other characteristics.



$$

y_j(n) = \sum_{i=1}^{N} w_{ji}(n) \cdot x_i(n)

$$



Figure 2: Small-signal model of the common-source configuration



The gain of the common-source configuration is given by the ratio of the output voltage to the input voltage, and it is typically denoted by the symbol A. The gain can be expressed as:



$$

A = \frac{v_{out}}{v_{in}}

$$



The input impedance of the common-source configuration is given by the ratio of the input voltage to the input current, and it is typically denoted by the symbol Zin. The input impedance can be expressed as:



$$

Z_{in} = \frac{v_{in}}{i_{in}}

$$



The output impedance of the common-source configuration is given by the ratio of the output voltage to the output current, and it is typically denoted by the symbol Zout. The output impedance can be expressed as:



$$

Z_{out} = \frac{v_{out}}{i_{out}}

$$



The common-source configuration has a wide range of applications in electronic circuits. It is commonly used as an amplifier, where it can amplify small signals to a larger magnitude. It is also used in oscillators, where it can generate a periodic output signal. Additionally, the common-source configuration is used in filters, where it can selectively pass or reject certain frequencies.



In conclusion, the common-source configuration is a fundamental building block stage in microelectronic devices and circuits. It has a high input impedance, low output impedance, and a wide range of applications. In the next section, we will explore the common-drain configuration, another type of single-transistor building block stage.

$$

However, it also has a limited bandwidth due to the presence of parasitic capacitances in the FET.



### Subsection: 7.1b Common-source in Single-transistor Building Block Stages

These stages are used to build more complex circuits and systems, and the common-source configuration is often used as a basic building block due to its simplicity and versatility.



One of the most common applications of the common-source configuration is in the design of operational amplifiers (op-amps). The common-source configuration is used as the input stage in op-amps, providing high input impedance and low output impedance, which are essential for accurate signal amplification.



Another application of the common-source configuration is in the design of analog-to-digital converters (ADCs). ADCs are used to convert analog signals, such as audio or video signals, into digital signals that can be processed by digital systems. The common-source configuration is used as the input stage in ADCs, providing high input impedance and low output impedance, which are necessary for accurate signal conversion.

In this role, it is used to match the impedance of a signal source to that of a load, ensuring maximum power transfer and minimal signal loss. It is also commonly used in active filters, where its frequency response can be tailored to achieve specific filtering characteristics.



In summary, the common-source configuration is a versatile and widely used building block stage in microelectronic devices and circuits. ## Chapter 7: Single-transistor Building Block Stages:



### Section: 7.2 Common-gate:



The common-gate configuration is another commonly used single-transistor building block stage in microelectronic devices and circuits. It is a type of amplifier that uses a field-effect transistor (FET) as its active device. In this section, we will introduce the common-gate configuration and discuss its characteristics and applications.



#### 7.2a Introduction to Common-gate



The common-gate configuration is a type of amplifier that uses a FET as its active device. It is called "common-gate" because the gate terminal of the FET is connected to the ground, which is a common reference point in electronic circuits. The input signal is applied to the source terminal, and the output signal is taken from the drain terminal. The common-gate configuration is shown in Figure 1.



$$

\Delta w = \eta \cdot x_j(n) \cdot y_j(n)

$$



Figure 1: Common-gate configuration



The common-gate configuration has a low input impedance, which means that it draws a significant amount of current from the input signal source. This makes it suitable for use in applications where the input signal source has a low output impedance. Additionally, the common-gate configuration has a high output impedance, which means that it can drive a load with a high impedance without significant signal loss.



The small-signal model of the common-gate configuration is shown in Figure 2. It consists of a voltage-controlled current source, representing the FET, and a load resistor, representing the output impedance of the circuit. The small-signal model is used to analyze the behavior of the circuit and determine its gain, input and output impedances, and other characteristics.



$$

y_j(n) = \sum_{i=1}^{N} w_{ji}(n) \cdot x_i(n)

$$



Figure 2: Small-signal model of the common-gate configuration



The gain of the common-gate configuration is given by the ratio of the output voltage to the input voltage, and it is typically less than 1. This means that the common-gate configuration acts as an attenuator, reducing the amplitude of the input signal. However, it also provides a high level of isolation between the input and output signals, making it useful in applications where signal isolation is important.



The common-gate configuration is commonly used in RF (radio frequency) circuits, as it provides good high-frequency performance and can be easily integrated into larger circuits. It is also used in low-noise amplifiers, where its low input impedance helps to reduce noise from the input signal source. Additionally, the common-gate configuration is often used as a buffer stage between different circuit blocks, as it can provide impedance matching and signal isolation.

## Chapter 7: Single-transistor Building Block Stages:



### Section: 7.2 Common-gate:



The common-gate configuration is a widely used single-transistor building block stage in microelectronic devices and circuits. It is a type of amplifier that utilizes a field-effect transistor (FET) as its active device. In this section, we will delve into the common-gate configuration and discuss its characteristics and applications.



#### 7.2b Common-gate in Single-transistor Building Block Stages



The common-gate configuration is an essential component in single-transistor building block stages. It is a type of amplifier that uses a FET as its active device. The gate terminal of the FET is connected to the ground, which is a common reference point in electronic circuits. The input signal is applied to the source terminal, and the output signal is taken from the drain terminal. The common-gate configuration is shown in Figure 1.



$$

\Delta w = \eta \cdot x_j(n) \cdot y_j(n)

$$



Figure 1: Common-gate configuration



The common-gate configuration has a low input impedance, which means that it draws a significant amount of current from the input signal source. This makes it suitable for use in applications where the input signal source has a low output impedance. Additionally, the common-gate configuration has a high output impedance, which means that it can drive a load with a high impedance without significant signal loss.



The small-signal model of the common-gate configuration is shown in Figure 2. It consists of a voltage-controlled current source, representing the FET, and a load resistor, representing the output impedance of the circuit. The small-signal model is used to analyze the behavior of the circuit and determine its gain, input and output impedances, and other characteristics.



$$

y_j(n) = \sum_{i=1}^{N} w_{ji}(n) \cdot x_i(n)

$$



Figure 2: Small-signal model of the common-gate configuration



The gain of the common-gate configuration is given by the ratio of the output voltage to the input voltage. It is influenced by the transconductance of the FET, which is the relationship between the input voltage and the output current. The transconductance is affected by the bias voltage applied to the FET, which can be adjusted to control the gain of the circuit.



One of the main applications of the common-gate configuration is in radio frequency (RF) amplifiers. Due to its high input impedance, it is suitable for amplifying weak RF signals without causing significant signal loss. It is also commonly used in low-noise amplifiers, which are essential in communication systems to minimize noise and interference.



Another application of the common-gate configuration is in mixers, which are used to convert signals from one frequency to another. The high output impedance of the common-gate configuration allows it to drive a load with a high impedance, making it suitable for use in mixers.



In conclusion, the common-gate configuration is a versatile building block stage that finds applications in various electronic circuits. Its unique characteristics make it suitable for use in RF amplifiers, mixers, and other applications where high input impedance and low signal loss are desired. In the next section, we will explore another important building block stage, the common-drain configuration.





## Chapter 7: Single-transistor Building Block Stages:



### Section: 7.3 Common-drain:



The common-drain configuration, also known as the source follower, is another important single-transistor building block stage in microelectronic devices and circuits. It is a type of amplifier that uses a field-effect transistor (FET) as its active device. In this section, we will explore the common-drain configuration and discuss its characteristics and applications.



#### 7.3a Introduction to Common-drain



The common-drain configuration is a type of amplifier that is commonly used in electronic circuits. It is a voltage buffer, which means that it has a high input impedance and a low output impedance. This makes it suitable for use in applications where the input signal source has a high output impedance and needs to be isolated from the load. The common-drain configuration is shown in Figure 1.



$$

\Delta w = \eta \cdot x_j(n) \cdot y_j(n)

$$



Figure 1: Common-drain configuration



The common-drain configuration has a low input impedance, which means that it draws a small amount of current from the input signal source. This makes it suitable for use in applications where the input signal source has a high output impedance. Additionally, the common-drain configuration has a high output impedance, which means that it can drive a load with a low impedance without significant signal loss.



The small-signal model of the common-drain configuration is shown in Figure 2. It consists of a voltage-controlled voltage source, representing the FET, and a load resistor, representing the output impedance of the circuit. The small-signal model is used to analyze the behavior of the circuit and determine its gain, input and output impedances, and other characteristics.



$$

y_j(n) = \sum_{i=1}^{N} w_{ji}(n) \cdot x_i(n)

$$



Figure 2: Small-signal model of the common-drain configuration



The gain of the common-drain configuration is given by the ratio of the output voltage to the input voltage. It is typically less than 1, which means that the output voltage is lower than the input voltage. This is due to the voltage drop across the load resistor. The input and output impedances of the common-drain configuration are determined by the FET and the load resistor, respectively.



The common-drain configuration is commonly used as a buffer in electronic circuits. It is also used in voltage regulators, where it provides a stable output voltage regardless of changes in the input voltage. Additionally, it is used in impedance matching circuits, where it matches the impedance of the input signal source to the impedance of the load. Overall, the common-drain configuration is a versatile building block stage that is essential in many electronic applications.





## Chapter 7: Single-transistor Building Block Stages:



### Section: 7.3 Common-drain:



The common-drain configuration, also known as the source follower, is another important single-transistor building block stage in microelectronic devices and circuits. It is a type of amplifier that uses a field-effect transistor (FET) as its active device. In this section, we will explore the common-drain configuration and discuss its characteristics and applications.



#### 7.3a Introduction to Common-drain



The common-drain configuration is a type of amplifier that is commonly used in electronic circuits. It is a voltage buffer, which means that it has a high input impedance and a low output impedance. This makes it suitable for use in applications where the input signal source has a high output impedance and needs to be isolated from the load. The common-drain configuration is shown in Figure 1.



$$

\Delta w = \eta \cdot x_j(n) \cdot y_j(n)

$$



Figure 1: Common-drain configuration



The common-drain configuration has a low input impedance, which means that it draws a small amount of current from the input signal source. This makes it suitable for use in applications where the input signal source has a high output impedance. Additionally, the common-drain configuration has a high output impedance, which means that it can drive a load with a low impedance without significant signal loss.



The small-signal model of the common-drain configuration is shown in Figure 2. It consists of a voltage-controlled voltage source, representing the FET, and a load resistor, representing the output impedance of the circuit. The small-signal model is used to analyze the behavior of the circuit and determine its gain, input and output impedances, and other characteristics.



$$

y_j(n) = \sum_{i=1}^{N} w_{ji}(n) \cdot x_i(n)

$$



Figure 2: Small-signal model of the common-drain configuration



The gain of the common-drain configuration is given by the ratio of the output voltage to the input voltage. This gain can be calculated using the small-signal model and is typically less than 1, meaning that the output voltage is lower than the input voltage. This is due to the voltage drop across the load resistor, which is necessary for the circuit to function as a buffer.



One of the main advantages of the common-drain configuration is its high input impedance. This allows it to be used in applications where the input signal source has a high output impedance, such as sensors or other electronic devices. The high input impedance also means that the circuit does not draw much current from the input source, making it more energy-efficient.



Another advantage of the common-drain configuration is its low output impedance. This allows it to drive a load with a low impedance without significant signal loss. This is useful in applications where the output signal needs to be transmitted over a long distance or to multiple devices.



The common-drain configuration is commonly used in electronic circuits as a voltage buffer, but it also has other applications. It can be used as a level shifter, converting a high voltage signal to a lower voltage signal. It can also be used as a current buffer, where the output current is equal to the input current. Additionally, the common-drain configuration can be used as a voltage regulator, maintaining a constant output voltage despite changes in the input voltage.



In conclusion, the common-drain configuration is an important single-transistor building block stage in microelectronic devices and circuits. Its high input impedance and low output impedance make it suitable for a variety of applications, including voltage buffering, level shifting, current buffering, and voltage regulation. Its small-signal model allows for easy analysis and optimization, making it a valuable tool for circuit designers. 





## Chapter 7: Single-transistor Building Block Stages:



### Section: 7.3 Common-drain:



The common-drain configuration, also known as the source follower, is another important single-transistor building block stage in microelectronic devices and circuits. It is a type of amplifier that uses a field-effect transistor (FET) as its active device. In this section, we will explore the common-drain configuration and discuss its characteristics and applications.



#### 7.3a Introduction to Common-drain



The common-drain configuration is a type of amplifier that is commonly used in electronic circuits. It is a voltage buffer, which means that it has a high input impedance and a low output impedance. This makes it suitable for use in applications where the input signal source has a high output impedance and needs to be isolated from the load. The common-drain configuration is shown in Figure 1.



$$

\Delta w = \eta \cdot x_j(n) \cdot y_j(n)

$$



Figure 1: Common-drain configuration



The common-drain configuration has a low input impedance, which means that it draws a small amount of current from the input signal source. This makes it suitable for use in applications where the input signal source has a high output impedance. Additionally, the common-drain configuration has a high output impedance, which means that it can drive a load with a low impedance without significant signal loss.



The small-signal model of the common-drain configuration is shown in Figure 2. It consists of a voltage-controlled voltage source, representing the FET, and a load resistor, representing the output impedance of the circuit. The small-signal model is used to analyze the behavior of the circuit and determine its gain, input and output impedances, and other characteristics.



$$

y_j(n) = \sum_{i=1}^{N} w_{ji}(n) \cdot x_i(n)

$$



Figure 2: Small-signal model of the common-drain configuration



The gain of the common-drain configuration is given by the ratio of the output voltage to the input voltage. It is typically less than 1, which means that the output voltage is lower than the input voltage. This is due to the voltage drop across the load resistor in the small-signal model. However, the common-drain configuration has a high voltage gain, which means that it can amplify small input signals to larger output signals.



#### 7.3b Characteristics of Common-drain



The common-drain configuration has several important characteristics that make it useful in various applications. These include:



- High input impedance: The input impedance of the common-drain configuration is typically high, which means that it draws a small amount of current from the input signal source. This makes it suitable for use in applications where the input signal source has a high output impedance.

- Low output impedance: The output impedance of the common-drain configuration is typically low, which means that it can drive a load with a low impedance without significant signal loss. This makes it suitable for use in applications where the load has a low impedance.

- High voltage gain: The common-drain configuration has a high voltage gain, which means that it can amplify small input signals to larger output signals. This makes it useful in applications where signal amplification is required.

- Low noise: The common-drain configuration has low noise characteristics, which means that it does not introduce significant noise into the circuit. This makes it suitable for use in applications where a clean signal is required.

- High bandwidth: The common-drain configuration has a high bandwidth, which means that it can handle a wide range of frequencies. This makes it suitable for use in applications where a wide range of frequencies need to be amplified.



#### 7.3c Applications of Common-drain



The common-drain configuration has various applications in microelectronic devices and circuits. Some of these include:



- Voltage buffer: The common-drain configuration is commonly used as a voltage buffer in electronic circuits. It isolates the input signal source from the load and provides a high input impedance and low output impedance.

- Level shifter: The common-drain configuration can also be used as a level shifter, converting a high voltage signal to a lower voltage signal. This is useful in applications where different voltage levels need to be interfaced.

- Amplifier: The common-drain configuration can be used as an amplifier in various electronic circuits. Its high voltage gain and low noise characteristics make it suitable for amplifying small input signals.

- Frequency mixer: The common-drain configuration can be used as a frequency mixer, combining two input signals to produce an output signal with a different frequency. This is useful in applications such as radio communication.

- Current source: The common-drain configuration can also be used as a current source, providing a constant current to a load. This is useful in applications where a stable current is required.



In conclusion, the common-drain configuration is an important building block stage in microelectronic devices and circuits. Its high input impedance, low output impedance, high voltage gain, low noise, and high bandwidth make it suitable for various applications such as voltage buffering, level shifting, amplification, frequency mixing, and current sourcing. 





## Chapter 7: Single-transistor Building Block Stages:



### Section: 7.4 Source-follower and Emitter-follower:



The source-follower and emitter-follower are two important single-transistor building block stages in microelectronic devices and circuits. They are both types of unity gain buffer amplifiers, meaning that they have a gain of slightly less than unity. In this section, we will explore the source-follower and emitter-follower configurations and discuss their characteristics and applications.



#### 7.4a Introduction to Source-follower and Emitter-follower



The source-follower and emitter-follower configurations are both types of voltage buffer amplifiers, similar to the common-drain configuration discussed in the previous section. They are commonly used in electronic circuits to isolate a high output impedance signal source from a low impedance load. The source-follower and emitter-follower configurations are shown in Figures 1 and 2, respectively.



$$

\Delta w = \eta \cdot x_j(n) \cdot y_j(n)

$$



Figure 1: Source-follower configuration



$$

y_j(n) = \sum_{i=1}^{N} w_{ji}(n) \cdot x_i(n)

$$



Figure 2: Emitter-follower configuration



The small-signal models of the source-follower and emitter-follower configurations are shown in Figures 3 and 4, respectively. They consist of a voltage-controlled voltage source, representing the transistor, and a load resistor, representing the output impedance of the circuit. The small-signal models are used to analyze the behavior of the circuits and determine their gains, input and output impedances, and other characteristics.



$$

y_j(n) = \sum_{i=1}^{N} w_{ji}(n) \cdot x_i(n)

$$



Figure 3: Small-signal model of the source-follower configuration



$$

y_j(n) = \sum_{i=1}^{N} w_{ji}(n) \cdot x_i(n)

$$



Figure 4: Small-signal model of the emitter-follower configuration



The source-follower and emitter-follower configurations have similar characteristics, with the main difference being the type of transistor used. The source-follower uses a field-effect transistor (FET) while the emitter-follower uses a bipolar junction transistor (BJT). Both configurations have a high input impedance and a low output impedance, making them suitable for use in applications where the input signal source has a high output impedance and needs to be isolated from the load.



The gain of the source-follower and emitter-follower configurations is slightly less than unity, but the difference is usually small and unimportant. This is due to the high input impedance of the circuits, which results in a small amount of current being drawn from the input signal source. Additionally, the output impedance of the circuits is low, allowing them to drive a load with a low impedance without significant signal loss.



In terms of impedance transformation, the source-follower and emitter-follower configurations have different effects. Using the small-signal circuit in Figure 5, the impedance seen looking into the circuit is increased by a factor of (β + 1) due to the added "r<sub>π</sub>". This is because the FET and BJT have different characteristics, with the FET having a higher input impedance and the BJT having a lower input impedance. As a result, the source-follower has a larger impedance transformation compared to the emitter-follower.



$$

y_j(n) = \sum_{i=1}^{N} w_{ji}(n) \cdot x_i(n)

$$



Figure 5: Small-signal model of the MOSFET voltage follower



In conclusion, the source-follower and emitter-follower configurations are important single-transistor building block stages in microelectronic devices and circuits. They are both types of unity gain buffer amplifiers with high input impedances and low output impedances, making them suitable for use in a variety of applications. While they have similar characteristics, the type of transistor used results in different impedance transformations. 





## Chapter 7: Single-transistor Building Block Stages:



### Section: 7.4 Source-follower and Emitter-follower:



In the previous section, we discussed the common-drain configuration, which is a type of unity gain buffer amplifier. In this section, we will explore two other important unity gain buffer amplifiers: the source-follower and emitter-follower configurations. These configurations are commonly used in electronic circuits to isolate a high output impedance signal source from a low impedance load.



#### 7.4a Introduction to Source-follower and Emitter-follower



The source-follower and emitter-follower configurations are both types of voltage buffer amplifiers, similar to the common-drain configuration. They are commonly used in electronic circuits to isolate a high output impedance signal source from a low impedance load. The source-follower and emitter-follower configurations are shown in Figures 1 and 2, respectively.



$$

\Delta w = \eta \cdot x_j(n) \cdot y_j(n)

$$



Figure 1: Source-follower configuration



$$

y_j(n) = \sum_{i=1}^{N} w_{ji}(n) \cdot x_i(n)

$$



Figure 2: Emitter-follower configuration



The small-signal models of the source-follower and emitter-follower configurations are shown in Figures 3 and 4, respectively. They consist of a voltage-controlled voltage source, representing the transistor, and a load resistor, representing the output impedance of the circuit. The small-signal models are used to analyze the behavior of the circuits and determine their gains, input and output impedances, and other characteristics.



$$

y_j(n) = \sum_{i=1}^{N} w_{ji}(n) \cdot x_i(n)

$$



Figure 3: Small-signal model of the source-follower configuration



$$

y_j(n) = \sum_{i=1}^{N} w_{ji}(n) \cdot x_i(n)

$$



Figure 4: Small-signal model of the emitter-follower configuration



The source-follower and emitter-follower configurations have similar characteristics, with the main difference being the type of transistor used. The source-follower uses a bipolar junction transistor (BJT) in common-collector configuration, while the emitter-follower uses a field effect transistor (FET) in common-drain configuration. Both configurations have a gain of slightly less than unity, but the difference is usually small and unimportant.



#### 7.4b Source-follower and Emitter-follower in Single-transistor Building Block Stages



In this subsection, we will discuss the use of source-follower and emitter-follower configurations in single-transistor building block stages. These configurations are commonly used as input stages in amplifiers, as they provide high input impedance and low output impedance, making them ideal for interfacing with different stages of a circuit.



The source-follower and emitter-follower configurations also have the advantage of impedance transformation. In the bipolar voltage follower, the impedance seen looking into the circuit is increased by a factor of (β + 1), where β is the transistor's current gain. This is significant because β is usually large, resulting in a substantial increase in impedance. Similarly, in the MOSFET voltage follower, the impedance seen looking into the circuit is infinite at low frequencies, making it an ideal buffer for high impedance sources.



However, as frequency increases, the parasitic capacitances of the transistors come into play, and the transformed input impedance drops. This effect is more pronounced in the MOSFET voltage follower due to the inherent capacitances of FETs.



In conclusion, the source-follower and emitter-follower configurations are important single-transistor building block stages that provide high input impedance and low output impedance. They are commonly used as input stages in amplifiers and have the added advantage of impedance transformation. However, their performance is affected by parasitic capacitances at higher frequencies, making them more suitable for low-frequency applications.

### Subsection: 7.4c Applications of Source-follower and Emitter-follower



The source-follower and emitter-follower configurations have a wide range of applications in microelectronic circuits. One common application is in audio amplifiers, where they are used to isolate the high impedance output of a microphone or guitar pickup from the low impedance input of a speaker or amplifier. They are also used in sensor circuits, where they can amplify the small output signals from sensors such as thermistors or photodiodes.



Another important application of the source-follower and emitter-follower configurations is in the design of operational amplifiers (op-amps). Op-amps are widely used in electronic circuits for tasks such as amplification, filtering, and signal conditioning. The source-follower and emitter-follower configurations are often used as the input and output stages of op-amps, providing high input and low output impedances to improve the overall performance of the circuit.



In addition, the source-follower and emitter-follower configurations have been used in the design of current conveyors, a type of circuit building block that can be used for a variety of signal processing tasks. They have also been used in the development of associative processors for data management and user interface management systems.



Overall, the source-follower and emitter-follower configurations are versatile and essential building blocks in the design of microelectronic circuits. Their high input impedance and low output impedance make them useful for a wide range of applications, and their simple design allows for easy integration into larger circuits. 





### Section: 7.5 Current Steering Logic:



Current steering logic is a type of digital logic that uses current rather than voltage to represent and manipulate binary signals. It is commonly used in integrated circuits for high-speed and low-power applications. In this section, we will discuss the basics of current steering logic and its applications in microelectronic devices and circuits.



#### 7.5a Introduction to Current Steering Logic



Current steering logic is a type of digital logic that uses the principle of current steering to manipulate binary signals. It is based on the concept of a current mirror, which is a circuit that replicates the current of one input to another output. In current steering logic, the input signal is converted into a current, which is then steered to different output paths based on the logic level of the input. This allows for fast and efficient switching between logic levels.



Current steering logic is commonly used in high-speed applications, such as in microprocessors and data communication systems. It offers several advantages over traditional voltage-based logic, including lower power consumption, faster switching speeds, and better noise immunity. Additionally, it is compatible with complementary metal-oxide-semiconductor (CMOS) technology, which is widely used in modern integrated circuits.



The basic building block of current steering logic is the current steering circuit, which consists of a current mirror and a switch. The current mirror replicates the input current, while the switch controls the direction of the current flow. By combining multiple current steering circuits, more complex logic functions can be implemented.



One of the key advantages of current steering logic is its ability to operate at low voltages. This is particularly useful in portable electronic devices, where power consumption is a critical factor. By using lower supply voltages, the power dissipation can be reduced, resulting in longer battery life.



In recent years, current steering logic has also been used in the development of artificial intelligence and machine learning systems. Its ability to efficiently process large amounts of data and perform complex calculations makes it a promising candidate for these applications.



In the next section, we will explore the different types of current steering logic and their applications in microelectronic devices and circuits. We will also discuss the challenges and future prospects of this technology. 





### Section: 7.5 Current Steering Logic:



Current steering logic is a type of digital logic that uses current rather than voltage to represent and manipulate binary signals. It is commonly used in integrated circuits for high-speed and low-power applications. In this section, we will discuss the basics of current steering logic and its applications in microelectronic devices and circuits.



#### 7.5a Introduction to Current Steering Logic



Current steering logic is a type of digital logic that uses the principle of current steering to manipulate binary signals. It is based on the concept of a current mirror, which is a circuit that replicates the current of one input to another output. In current steering logic, the input signal is converted into a current, which is then steered to different output paths based on the logic level of the input. This allows for fast and efficient switching between logic levels.



Current steering logic is commonly used in high-speed applications, such as in microprocessors and data communication systems. It offers several advantages over traditional voltage-based logic, including lower power consumption, faster switching speeds, and better noise immunity. Additionally, it is compatible with complementary metal-oxide-semiconductor (CMOS) technology, which is widely used in modern integrated circuits.



The basic building block of current steering logic is the current steering circuit, which consists of a current mirror and a switch. The current mirror replicates the input current, while the switch controls the direction of the current flow. By combining multiple current steering circuits, more complex logic functions can be implemented.



One of the key advantages of current steering logic is its ability to operate at low voltages. This is particularly useful in portable electronic devices, where power consumption is a critical factor. By using lower supply voltages, the power dissipation can be reduced, resulting in longer battery life.



#### 7.5b Current Steering Logic in Single-transistor Building Block Stages



In the previous section, we discussed the basics of current steering logic and its applications in digital circuits. In this section, we will focus on the use of current steering logic in single-transistor building block stages.



Single-transistor building block stages are commonly used in integrated circuits for their simplicity and low power consumption. They consist of a single MOSFET (metal-oxide-semiconductor field-effect transistor) and a few passive components. These stages are used to perform basic logic functions, such as inversion, amplification, and switching.



Current steering logic is particularly useful in single-transistor building block stages because it allows for efficient and fast switching between logic levels. By using a current mirror and a switch, the input current can be steered to different output paths, allowing for the implementation of various logic functions.



One example of current steering logic in single-transistor building block stages is the use of a current steering circuit to perform inversion. By using a current mirror to replicate the input current and a switch to control the direction of the current flow, the output can be inverted. This is achieved by connecting the output to the input of the current mirror, which will result in an inverted output.



Another example is the use of current steering logic to perform amplification. By using a current mirror to replicate the input current and a switch to control the direction of the current flow, the output can be amplified. This is achieved by connecting the output to the input of the current mirror, which will result in a larger output current.



In addition to performing basic logic functions, current steering logic can also be used to improve the performance of single-transistor building block stages. By using current steering, the input impedance of the stage can be increased, resulting in better noise immunity and reduced loading effects between logic stages.



In conclusion, current steering logic is a powerful tool in the design of single-transistor building block stages. Its ability to efficiently and quickly switch between logic levels makes it a valuable technique in high-speed and low-power applications. By using current steering, the performance of single-transistor building block stages can be improved, making it an essential concept in the field of microelectronic devices and circuits.

#### 7.5b Current Steering Logic in Microelectronic Devices

One of the key applications of current steering logic is in the design of microprocessors, where it is used to implement complex logic functions with high speed and efficiency.



In microprocessors, current steering logic is used to implement arithmetic and logic operations, such as addition, subtraction, and logical AND, OR, and NOT operations. By using current steering logic, these operations can be performed with minimal delay and power consumption, making it ideal for high-performance computing.

#### 7.5c Applications of Current Steering Logic



In addition to microprocessors and data communication systems, current steering logic has also found applications in other areas of microelectronics. One such application is in the design of memory circuits, where it is used to implement high-speed and low-power memory cells.



Current steering logic is also used in the design of analog-to-digital converters (ADCs) and digital-to-analog converters (DACs). By using current steering logic, these converters can achieve high-speed and high-precision conversion, making them suitable for applications such as audio and video processing.



Furthermore, current steering logic has also been used in the design of sensors and actuators, where it is used to convert analog signals into digital signals and vice versa. By using current steering logic, these devices can achieve high-speed and high-precision operation, making them suitable for a wide range of applications.



In conclusion, current steering logic is a versatile and powerful tool in the design of microelectronic devices and circuits. Its ability to operate at low voltages, high speeds, and low power consumption makes it an essential component in modern integrated circuits. As technology continues to advance, we can expect to see even more applications of current steering logic in various fields of microelectronics.





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fundamentals of single-transistor building block stages in microelectronic devices and circuits. We have discussed the different types of single-transistor stages, including common-source, common-drain, and common-gate configurations, and their corresponding characteristics and applications. We have also delved into the analysis and design of these stages, covering topics such as biasing, small-signal analysis, and frequency response.



Through this chapter, we have gained a deeper understanding of the role of single-transistor stages in microelectronic devices and circuits. These stages serve as the basic building blocks for more complex circuits, and their proper design and analysis are crucial for the overall performance of the system. By mastering the concepts and techniques presented in this chapter, readers will be well-equipped to tackle more advanced topics in microelectronics.



In conclusion, single-transistor building block stages are essential components in microelectronic devices and circuits. They provide the necessary amplification and signal processing capabilities that enable the functionality of complex systems. With the knowledge gained from this chapter, readers will be able to apply these concepts to real-world applications and continue their journey towards becoming experts in microelectronics.

#### Exercise 1

Given a common-source amplifier with a drain current of $I_D = 2mA$ and a transconductance of $g_m = 0.5mS$, calculate the output voltage for an input voltage of $V_{in} = 1V$.



#### Exercise 2

Design a common-drain amplifier with a gain of 10 and a bias current of $I_D = 1mA$. Assume a supply voltage of $V_{DD} = 5V$ and a transistor with a transconductance of $g_m = 0.2mS$.



#### Exercise 3

Using the small-signal model, derive an expression for the voltage gain of a common-gate amplifier in terms of the transistor parameters $g_m$ and $r_o$.



#### Exercise 4

Investigate the frequency response of a common-source amplifier by plotting the gain and phase shift as a function of frequency. Use the following parameters: $R_S = 1k\Omega$, $R_D = 5k\Omega$, $C_{gs} = 1pF$, $C_{gd} = 0.5pF$, $g_m = 0.5mS$, and $r_o = 10k\Omega$.



#### Exercise 5

Design a cascode amplifier using a common-source stage and a common-gate stage. The overall gain should be 100 and the bias current should be $I_D = 2mA$. Assume a supply voltage of $V_{DD} = 10V$ and transistors with $g_m = 0.5mS$ and $r_o = 10k\Omega$.

# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 8: Differential Amplifiers



### Section 8.1: Large Signal Transfer Characteristics



In this section, we will discuss the large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers. These characteristics are essential in understanding the behavior and performance of differential amplifiers in various applications.



#### 8.1a: Introduction to Large Signal Transfer Characteristics



Before we dive into the specifics of large signal transfer characteristics, let's first define what we mean by "large signal." In the context of differential amplifiers, a large signal refers to an input signal that is significantly larger than the amplifier's small-signal operating range. This means that the amplifier is operating in a nonlinear region, and the output signal is no longer a linear function of the input signal.



To understand the large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers, we must first understand the concept of distortion. Distortion refers to any deviation from the ideal output signal that is caused by the nonlinear behavior of the amplifier. In other words, distortion is the difference between the actual output signal and the ideal output signal that we would expect from a linear amplifier.



The large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers are typically represented by plots of the output voltage versus the input voltage. These plots are known as transfer curves and can provide valuable insights into the amplifier's behavior. The shape of the transfer curve can reveal information about the amplifier's gain, linearity, and distortion.



In the traditional derivation of one-dimensional digital pre-distortion (1DDPD), a single input signal is used. However, in the derivation of two-dimensional digital pre-distortion (2DDPD), the input to the nonlinear system is replaced with the summation of two orthogonal signals. These signals are orthogonal because they are frequency translated by ω<sub>1</sub> and ω<sub>2</sub>, which are carefully selected to ensure channel orthogonality.



The resulting transfer curve for a 2DDPD system will have both in-band and out-of-band terms. The in-band terms come from the expansion of the polynomials in a traditional 1DDPD manner, where the first, third, and fifth order coefficients are considered coupled or non-orthogonal. On the other hand, the out-of-band terms come from the polynomial expansion in a traditional 1DDPD manner, where the first, third, and fifth order coefficients are considered uncoupled or orthogonal.



The use of 2DDPD allows for better control and correction of nonlinearities in the amplifier, resulting in improved large signal transfer characteristics. This technique has been successfully applied in various applications, such as wireless communication systems and power amplifiers.



In conclusion, understanding the large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers is crucial in designing and optimizing their performance in various applications. The use of techniques like 2DDPD can greatly improve the amplifier's linearity and reduce distortion, making it an essential tool in modern microelectronic devices and circuits. 





# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 8: Differential Amplifiers



### Section 8.1: Large Signal Transfer Characteristics



In this section, we will discuss the large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers. These characteristics are essential in understanding the behavior and performance of differential amplifiers in various applications.



#### 8.1a: Introduction to Large Signal Transfer Characteristics
