This means that the amplifier is operating in a nonlinear region, and the output signal is no longer a linear function of the input signal.



To understand the large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers, we must first understand the concept of distortion. Distortion refers to any deviation from the ideal output signal that is caused by the nonlinear behavior of the amplifier. In other words, distortion is the difference between the actual output signal and the ideal output signal that we would expect from a linear amplifier.



The large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers are typically represented by plots of the output voltage versus the input voltage. These plots are known as transfer curves and can provide valuable insights into the amplifier's behavior. The shape of the transfer curve can reveal information about the amplifier's gain, linearity, and distortion.



In the traditional derivation of one-dimensional digital pre-distortion (1DDPD), a single input signal is used. However, in the derivation of two-dimensional digital pre-distortion (2DDPD), the input to the nonlinear system is replaced with the summation of two orthogonal signals. These signals are orthogonal because they are frequency translated by ω<sub>1</sub> and ω<sub>2</sub>, which are carefully selected to ensure channel orthogonality.



The use of two orthogonal signals in 2DDPD allows for a more accurate representation of the nonlinear behavior of the amplifier. This is because the traditional 1DDPD approach only considers the first, third, and fifth order coefficients as coupled or non-orthogonal, while in 2DDPD, all coefficients are considered uncoupled or orthogonal. This results in a more comprehensive understanding of the amplifier's large signal transfer characteristics.



In addition to the in-band terms that come from the expansion of the polynomials in the traditional 1DDPD manner, 2DDPD also considers out-of-band terms. These terms, represented by Equations ((<EquationNote|5>)),((<EquationNote|6>)),((<EquationNote|7>)),((<EquationNote|8>)), ((<EquationNote|9>)), and ((<EquationNote|10>)), provide a more complete picture of the amplifier's behavior, including its performance in frequencies outside of the operating range.



Overall, the use of two orthogonal signals in 2DDPD allows for a more accurate and comprehensive understanding of the large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers. This is crucial in designing and optimizing these amplifiers for various applications. 





# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 8: Differential Amplifiers



### Section 8.1: Large Signal Transfer Characteristics



In this section, we will discuss the large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers. These characteristics are essential in understanding the behavior and performance of differential amplifiers in various applications.



#### 8.1a: Introduction to Large Signal Transfer Characteristics



Before we dive into the specifics of large signal transfer characteristics, let's first define what we mean by "large signal." In the context of differential amplifiers, a large signal refers to an input signal that is significantly larger than the amplifier's small-signal operating range. This means that the amplifier is operating in a nonlinear region, and the output signal is no longer a linear function of the input signal.



To understand the large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers, we must first understand the concept of distortion. Distortion refers to any deviation from the ideal output signal that is caused by the nonlinear behavior of the amplifier. In other words, distortion is the difference between the actual output signal and the ideal output signal that we would expect from a linear amplifier.



The large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers are typically represented by plots of the output voltage versus the input voltage. These plots are known as transfer curves and can provide valuable insights into the amplifier's behavior. The shape of the transfer curve can reveal information about the amplifier's gain, linearity, and distortion.



In the traditional derivation of one-dimensional digital pre-distortion (1DDPD), a single input signal is used. However, in the derivation of two-dimensional digital pre-distortion (2DDPD), the input to the nonlinear system is replaced with the summation of two orthogonal signals. These signals are orthogonal because they are independent and do not affect each other. This approach allows for a more accurate representation of the nonlinear behavior of the amplifier, as it takes into account the interaction between the two input signals.



#### 8.1b: Nonlinear Behavior of Differential Amplifiers



As mentioned earlier, when a differential amplifier is operating in a large signal regime, its behavior becomes nonlinear. This means that the output signal is no longer a linear function of the input signal. Instead, the output signal is affected by various nonlinearities, such as saturation, clipping, and distortion.



Saturation occurs when the input signal is too large, and the amplifier reaches its maximum output voltage. In this case, the output signal remains constant, even if the input signal continues to increase. Clipping, on the other hand, occurs when the input signal exceeds the amplifier's supply voltage, causing the output signal to be clipped or "cut off" at the maximum or minimum voltage.



Distortion, as mentioned earlier, refers to any deviation from the ideal output signal. This can be caused by various factors, such as nonlinearities in the amplifier's components or mismatches between the two input signals.



#### 8.1c: Applications of Large Signal Transfer Characteristics



The large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers have various applications in the field of microelectronics. One of the most common applications is in the design of power amplifiers for wireless communication systems. In these systems, the input signals are typically large, and the amplifier must operate in a nonlinear regime to achieve high power efficiency.



Another application is in the design of analog-to-digital converters (ADCs). ADCs are used to convert analog signals, such as audio or video, into digital signals that can be processed by digital systems. The large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers play a crucial role in the design of high-performance ADCs, as they affect the accuracy and linearity of the conversion process.



In conclusion, understanding the large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers is essential in the design and analysis of various microelectronic systems. These characteristics provide valuable insights into the nonlinear behavior of the amplifier and can be used to optimize its performance in different applications. 





# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 8: Differential Amplifiers



### Section: 8.2 Small Signal Analysis



In the previous section, we discussed the large signal transfer characteristics of differential amplifiers. Now, we will shift our focus to small signal analysis, which is essential in understanding the behavior and performance of differential amplifiers in linear applications.



#### 8.2a: Introduction to Small Signal Analysis



Small signal analysis is a technique used to analyze the behavior of electronic circuits when the input signal is small enough to be considered a perturbation on top of the DC operating point. In the context of differential amplifiers, this means that the input signal is small enough to be within the linear operating range of the amplifier.



To understand small signal analysis, we must first understand the concept of linearity. A linear system is one in which the output is directly proportional to the input. In other words, if we double the input signal, the output signal will also double. This is in contrast to a nonlinear system, where the output is not directly proportional to the input.



In small signal analysis, we assume that the amplifier is operating in a linear region, and the output signal is a linear function of the input signal. This allows us to use linear algebra techniques to analyze the circuit's behavior and calculate important parameters such as gain, input and output impedances, and frequency response.



One of the key advantages of small signal analysis is that it allows us to simplify complex circuits into simpler models that are easier to analyze. This is particularly useful in the design and optimization of electronic circuits, as it allows us to quickly evaluate different circuit configurations and choose the most suitable one for a given application.



In the next section, we will dive deeper into the techniques and equations used in small signal analysis and explore their applications in differential amplifiers.

#### 8.2a: Introduction to Small Signal Analysis



Small signal analysis is a technique used to analyze the behavior of electronic circuits when the input signal is small enough to be considered a perturbation on top of the DC operating point. In the context of differential amplifiers, this means that the input signal is small enough to be within the linear operating range of the amplifier.



To understand small signal analysis, we must first understand the concept of linearity. A linear system is one in which the output is directly proportional to the input. This allows us to use linear algebra techniques to analyze the circuit's behavior and calculate important parameters such as gain, input and output impedances, and frequency response.

In this section, we will focus on small signal analysis in differential amplifiers. We will start by discussing the small signal model of a differential amplifier and then move on to analyzing its behavior using linear algebra techniques.



### Subsection: 8.2b Small Signal Analysis in Differential Amplifiers



In order to perform small signal analysis on a differential amplifier, we first need to develop a small signal model for the amplifier. This model will allow us to analyze the amplifier's behavior using linear algebra techniques.



The small signal model of a differential amplifier consists of two parts: the input stage and the output stage. The input stage is responsible for amplifying the differential input signal, while the output stage is responsible for converting the amplified signal into a single-ended output.



To develop the small signal model, we will use the concept of incremental analysis. This means that we will analyze the circuit by considering small changes in the input and output signals, rather than the actual signals themselves. This allows us to simplify the analysis and focus on the linear behavior of the circuit.



Using incremental analysis, we can derive the small signal equations for the input and output stages of the differential amplifier. These equations will allow us to calculate important parameters such as the differential gain, common-mode gain, input and output impedances, and frequency response.



In the next section, we will dive deeper into the small signal equations and explore their applications in analyzing the behavior of differential amplifiers. We will also discuss the limitations of small signal analysis and how to overcome them in practical circuit design.

# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 8: Differential Amplifiers



### Section: 8.3 Common- and Difference-mode Inputs



In the previous section, we discussed the small signal analysis of differential amplifiers. Now, we will shift our focus to the inputs of differential amplifiers and how they affect the overall performance of the amplifier. In this section, we will specifically look at common- and difference-mode inputs and their impact on the amplifier's output.



#### 8.3a: Introduction to Common- and Difference-mode Inputs



In differential amplifiers, there are two types of inputs: common-mode and difference-mode. Common-mode inputs are those that are applied to both inputs of the amplifier, while difference-mode inputs are applied to only one input. These inputs play a crucial role in determining the amplifier's output and its ability to reject unwanted signals.



To understand the difference between common- and difference-mode inputs, we must first understand the concept of common-mode rejection ratio (CMRR). CMRR is a measure of the amplifier's ability to reject common-mode signals and amplify only the difference-mode signals. A high CMRR is desirable in differential amplifiers as it indicates a high level of rejection of unwanted signals.



In order to achieve a high CMRR, the amplifier must have a balanced design, meaning that the two input transistors must be matched in terms of their characteristics. This ensures that any common-mode signal applied to both inputs will be cancelled out, leaving only the difference-mode signal to be amplified.



In this section, we will delve deeper into the effects of common- and difference-mode inputs on the performance of differential amplifiers. We will also discuss techniques for improving CMRR and achieving a more balanced design. 





# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 8: Differential Amplifiers



### Section: 8.3 Common- and Difference-mode Inputs



In the previous section, we discussed the small signal analysis of differential amplifiers. Now, we will shift our focus to the inputs of differential amplifiers and how they affect the overall performance of the amplifier. In this section, we will specifically look at common- and difference-mode inputs and their impact on the amplifier's output.



#### 8.3a: Introduction to Common- and Difference-mode Inputs



In differential amplifiers, there are two types of inputs: common-mode and difference-mode. Common-mode inputs are those that are applied to both inputs of the amplifier, while difference-mode inputs are applied to only one input. These inputs play a crucial role in determining the amplifier's output and its ability to reject unwanted signals.



To understand the difference between common- and difference-mode inputs, we must first understand the concept of common-mode rejection ratio (CMRR). CMRR is a measure of the amplifier's ability to reject common-mode signals and amplify only the difference-mode signals. A high CMRR is desirable in differential amplifiers as it indicates a high level of rejection of unwanted signals.



In order to achieve a high CMRR, the amplifier must have a balanced design, meaning that the two input transistors must be matched in terms of their characteristics. This ensures that any common-mode signal applied to both inputs will be cancelled out, leaving only the difference-mode signal to be amplified.



### Subsection: 8.3b Common- and Difference-mode Inputs in Differential Amplifiers



In the previous section, we discussed the importance of achieving a balanced design in differential amplifiers to improve CMRR. Now, we will delve deeper into the effects of common- and difference-mode inputs on the performance of differential amplifiers.



When a common-mode input is applied to both inputs of the amplifier, it results in a common-mode output. This output is undesirable as it does not contain any useful information and can potentially interfere with the desired difference-mode output. Therefore, it is important to minimize the common-mode gain of the amplifier.



On the other hand, when a difference-mode input is applied to only one input of the amplifier, it results in a difference-mode output. This output is the desired output of the amplifier and contains the useful information. Therefore, it is important to maximize the difference-mode gain of the amplifier.



To achieve a high CMRR, the common-mode gain must be minimized and the difference-mode gain must be maximized. This can be achieved by using a symmetrical feedback network that eliminates common-mode gain and common-mode bias. This network consists of current generators that model the input bias current at each terminal.



The Thévenin equivalent for the network driving the "V"<sup>+</sup> terminal has a voltage "V"<sup>+</sup>' and impedance "R"<sup>+</sup>':

while for the network driving the "V"<sup>−</sup> terminal:

The output of the op-amp is just the open-loop gain "A"<sub>ol</sub> times the differential input current "i" times the differential input impedance 2"R"<sub>d</sub>, therefore

where "R"<sub>||</sub> is the average of "R"<sup>+</sup><sub>||</sub> and "R"<sup>−</sup><sub>||</sub>.



These equations undergo a great simplification if 

resulting in the relation

which implies that the closed-loop gain for the differential signal is "V"<sup>+</sup><sub>in</sub> − "V"<sup>−</sup><sub>in</sub>, but the common-mode gain is identically zero. 



It also implies that the common-mode input bias current has cancelled out, leaving only the input offset current "I"<sup>Δ</sup><sub>b</sub> = "I"<sup>+</sup><sub>b</sub> − "I"<sup>−</sup><sub>b</sub> still present, and with a coefficient of "R"<sub>i</sub>. It is as if the input offset current is equivalent to an input offset voltage acting across an input resistance "R"<sub>i</sub>, which is the source resistance of the feedback network into the input terminals.



Finally, as long as the open-loop voltage gain "A"<sub>ol</sub> is much larger than unity, the closed-loop voltage gain is "R"<sub>f</sub>/"R"<sub>i</sub>, the value one would obtain through the rule-of-thumb analysis known as the "golden rule". This shows the importance of a high open-loop gain in achieving a high CMRR in differential amplifiers.



In conclusion, common- and difference-mode inputs play a crucial role in the performance of differential amplifiers. By using a balanced design and a symmetrical feedback network, we can minimize the common-mode gain and maximize the difference-mode gain, resulting in a high CMRR and improved overall performance of the amplifier.

## Chapter 8: Differential Amplifiers



### Section: 8.3 Common- and Difference-mode Inputs



In the previous section, we discussed the small signal analysis of differential amplifiers. Now, we will shift our focus to the inputs of differential amplifiers and how they affect the overall performance of the amplifier. In this section, we will specifically look at common- and difference-mode inputs and their impact on the amplifier's output.



#### 8.3a: Introduction to Common- and Difference-mode Inputs



In differential amplifiers, there are two types of inputs: common-mode and difference-mode. Common-mode inputs are those that are applied to both inputs of the amplifier, while difference-mode inputs are applied to only one input. These inputs play a crucial role in determining the amplifier's output and its ability to reject unwanted signals.



To understand the difference between common- and difference-mode inputs, we must first understand the concept of common-mode rejection ratio (CMRR). CMRR is a measure of the amplifier's ability to reject common-mode signals and amplify only the difference-mode signals. A high CMRR is desirable in differential amplifiers as it indicates a high level of rejection of unwanted signals.



In order to achieve a high CMRR, the amplifier must have a balanced design, meaning that the two input transistors must be matched in terms of their characteristics. This ensures that any common-mode signal applied to both inputs will be cancelled out, leaving only the difference-mode signal to be amplified.



### Subsection: 8.3b Common- and Difference-mode Inputs in Differential Amplifiers



In the previous section, we discussed the importance of achieving a balanced design in differential amplifiers to improve CMRR. Now, we will delve deeper into the effects of common- and difference-mode inputs on the performance of differential amplifiers.



When a common-mode input is applied to both inputs of the amplifier, the output will be zero. This is because the two input transistors are matched and any common-mode signal will be cancelled out. However, when a difference-mode input is applied, the output will be amplified.



One application of common- and difference-mode inputs is in noise cancellation. By applying a common-mode input to both inputs of the amplifier, any noise present in the signal will be cancelled out, leaving only the desired signal to be amplified. This is especially useful in high precision applications where even small amounts of noise can affect the accuracy of the output.



Another application is in instrumentation amplifiers, where the difference-mode input is used to amplify small differential signals, while the common-mode input is used to reject any common-mode noise. This allows for a high CMRR and accurate amplification of the desired signal.



In conclusion, common- and difference-mode inputs play a crucial role in the performance of differential amplifiers. By achieving a balanced design and utilizing these inputs effectively, we can improve the amplifier's ability to reject unwanted signals and amplify the desired signal accurately. 





# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 8: Differential Amplifiers



### Section: 8.4 Active Load Differential Amplifiers



In the previous section, we discussed the importance of achieving a balanced design in differential amplifiers to improve CMRR. Now, we will delve deeper into the effects of common- and difference-mode inputs on the performance of differential amplifiers.



Active load differential amplifiers are a type of differential amplifier that use active components, such as transistors, as the load for the differential pair. This allows for a higher gain and better performance compared to passive load differential amplifiers.



#### 8.4a: Introduction to Active Load Differential Amplifiers



Active load differential amplifiers have become increasingly popular due to their ability to provide high gain and improved CMRR. In these amplifiers, the load is replaced by an active component, typically a transistor, which allows for a larger output resistance and thus a higher gain.



One of the main advantages of active load differential amplifiers is their ability to provide a high CMRR. This is due to the fact that the active load can be designed to have a high output resistance, which helps in rejecting common-mode signals. Additionally, the use of active components allows for better matching between the two input transistors, leading to a more balanced design and further improving CMRR.



### Subsection: 8.4b Design and Analysis of Active Load Differential Amplifiers



To design an active load differential amplifier, the first step is to select the type of active load to be used. This can include a common-emitter, common-collector, or common-base configuration. The choice of active load will depend on the desired gain and other performance requirements.



Once the active load is selected, the next step is to analyze the circuit using small signal analysis techniques. This involves determining the small signal parameters, such as the input resistance, output resistance, and voltage gain. These parameters can then be used to optimize the circuit for the desired performance.



One of the key advantages of active load differential amplifiers is their ability to provide a high gain. This is due to the fact that the active load can be designed to have a high output resistance, which helps in amplifying the difference-mode signal. Additionally, the use of active components allows for better matching between the two input transistors, leading to a more balanced design and further improving the gain.



In conclusion, active load differential amplifiers offer significant advantages over passive load differential amplifiers, including improved CMRR and higher gain. These amplifiers have become an essential component in modern microelectronic devices and circuits, and their design and analysis are crucial for understanding their performance. 





# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 8: Differential Amplifiers



### Section: 8.4 Active Load Differential Amplifiers



In the previous section, we discussed the importance of achieving a balanced design in differential amplifiers to improve CMRR. Now, we will delve deeper into the effects of common- and difference-mode inputs on the performance of differential amplifiers.



Active load differential amplifiers are a type of differential amplifier that use active components, such as transistors, as the load for the differential pair. This allows for a higher gain and better performance compared to passive load differential amplifiers.



#### 8.4a: Introduction to Active Load Differential Amplifiers



Active load differential amplifiers have become increasingly popular due to their ability to provide high gain and improved CMRR. In these amplifiers, the load is replaced by an active component, typically a transistor, which allows for a larger output resistance and thus a higher gain.



One of the main advantages of active load differential amplifiers is their ability to provide a high CMRR. This is due to the fact that the active load can be designed to have a high output resistance, which helps in rejecting common-mode signals. Additionally, the use of active components allows for better matching between the two input transistors, leading to a more balanced design and further improving CMRR.



### Subsection: 8.4b Design and Analysis of Active Load Differential Amplifiers



To design an active load differential amplifier, the first step is to select the type of active load to be used. This can include a common-emitter, common-collector, or common-base configuration. The choice of active load will depend on the desired gain and other performance requirements.



Once the active load is selected, the next step is to analyze the circuit using small signal analysis techniques. This involves determining the small signal parameters, such as the input resistance, output resistance, and voltage gain. These parameters can be calculated using the hybrid-pi model, which represents the small signal behavior of a transistor.



The voltage gain of an active load differential amplifier can be expressed as:



$$

A_v = \frac{R_{out}}{R_{in}} \cdot g_m \cdot R_L

$$



Where $R_{out}$ is the output resistance of the active load, $R_{in}$ is the input resistance of the differential pair, $g_m$ is the transconductance of the input transistors, and $R_L$ is the load resistance.



The choice of load resistance is crucial in determining the gain of the amplifier. A higher load resistance will result in a higher gain, but it also increases the output impedance and can lead to stability issues. Therefore, a trade-off must be made between gain and stability when selecting the load resistance.



Another important consideration in the design of active load differential amplifiers is the biasing of the transistors. The biasing circuit must be designed to ensure that the transistors operate in the desired region, typically the active region, to achieve the desired gain and linearity.



In conclusion, active load differential amplifiers offer several advantages over passive load differential amplifiers, including higher gain and improved CMRR. However, careful design and analysis are required to ensure proper operation and stability of the amplifier. 





# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 8: Differential Amplifiers



### Section: 8.4 Active Load Differential Amplifiers



In the previous section, we discussed the importance of achieving a balanced design in differential amplifiers to improve CMRR. Now, we will delve deeper into the effects of common- and difference-mode inputs on the performance of differential amplifiers.



Active load differential amplifiers are a type of differential amplifier that use active components, such as transistors, as the load for the differential pair. This allows for a higher gain and better performance compared to passive load differential amplifiers.



#### 8.4a: Introduction to Active Load Differential Amplifiers



Active load differential amplifiers have become increasingly popular due to their ability to provide high gain and improved CMRR. In these amplifiers, the load is replaced by an active component, typically a transistor, which allows for a larger output resistance and thus a higher gain.



One of the main advantages of active load differential amplifiers is their ability to provide a high CMRR. This is due to the fact that the active load can be designed to have a high output resistance, which helps in rejecting common-mode signals. Additionally, the use of active components allows for better matching between the two input transistors, leading to a more balanced design and further improving CMRR.



### Subsection: 8.4b Design and Analysis of Active Load Differential Amplifiers



To design an active load differential amplifier, the first step is to select the type of active load to be used. This can include a common-emitter, common-collector, or common-base configuration. The choice of active load will depend on the desired gain and other performance requirements.



Once the active load is selected, the next step is to analyze the circuit using small signal analysis techniques. This involves determining the small signal parameters, such as the input resistance, output resistance, and voltage gain. These parameters can be calculated using the hybrid-pi model for transistors.



The design of an active load differential amplifier also involves selecting appropriate biasing and compensation techniques to ensure stable and linear operation. This may include using current mirrors, cascode configurations, and feedback networks.



### Subsection: 8.4c Applications of Active Load Differential Amplifiers



Active load differential amplifiers have a wide range of applications in microelectronic devices and circuits. One common application is in operational amplifiers, where active load differential amplifiers are used as the input stage to provide high gain and low distortion.



Another application is in analog-to-digital converters (ADCs), where active load differential amplifiers are used as the input stage to amplify and condition the analog signal before it is converted to a digital signal. The high gain and low distortion provided by active load differential amplifiers are crucial for accurate and reliable conversion.



Active load differential amplifiers are also used in communication systems, such as in radio frequency (RF) amplifiers, to provide high gain and low noise performance. They are also used in high-speed data transmission systems, where they can provide high bandwidth and low distortion.



In conclusion, active load differential amplifiers are a versatile and essential component in microelectronic devices and circuits. Their ability to provide high gain, low distortion, and improved CMRR make them a valuable tool for a wide range of applications. 





# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 8: Differential Amplifiers



### Section: 8.5 Cascode Differential Amplifiers



In the previous section, we discussed the design and analysis of active load differential amplifiers. Now, we will explore another type of differential amplifier - the cascode differential amplifier.



#### 8.5a Introduction to Cascode Differential Amplifiers



Cascode differential amplifiers are a type of differential amplifier that use a cascode configuration, where two transistors are connected in series, as the load for the differential pair. This configuration allows for a higher gain and improved performance compared to other types of differential amplifiers.



One of the main advantages of cascode differential amplifiers is their ability to provide a high output resistance, which helps in rejecting common-mode signals. This is due to the fact that the cascode configuration effectively increases the output resistance of the differential pair. Additionally, the use of two transistors in series allows for better matching between the two input transistors, leading to a more balanced design and further improving CMRR.



### Subsection: 8.5b Design and Analysis of Cascode Differential Amplifiers



To design a cascode differential amplifier, the first step is to select the type of transistors to be used in the cascode configuration. This can include bipolar junction transistors (BJTs) or metal-oxide-semiconductor field-effect transistors (MOSFETs). The choice of transistors will depend on the desired gain and other performance requirements.



Once the transistors are selected, the next step is to analyze the circuit using small signal analysis techniques. This involves determining the small signal parameters, such as the transconductance and output resistance, of each transistor in the cascode configuration. These parameters will then be used to calculate the overall gain and other performance metrics of the cascode differential amplifier.



One of the key design challenges for cascode differential amplifiers is maintaining a high switching speed. This is because the cascode configuration can introduce additional capacitance and delay in the circuit, which can affect the overall performance. Careful consideration must be given to the selection of transistors and the layout of the circuit to minimize these effects.



In conclusion, cascode differential amplifiers offer a high gain and improved CMRR compared to other types of differential amplifiers. However, careful design and analysis are necessary to ensure optimal performance and address potential challenges. 





# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 8: Differential Amplifiers



### Section: 8.5 Cascode Differential Amplifiers



In the previous section, we discussed the design and analysis of active load differential amplifiers. Now, we will explore another type of differential amplifier - the cascode differential amplifier.



#### 8.5a Introduction to Cascode Differential Amplifiers



Cascode differential amplifiers are a type of differential amplifier that use a cascode configuration, where two transistors are connected in series, as the load for the differential pair. This configuration allows for a higher gain and improved performance compared to other types of differential amplifiers.



One of the main advantages of cascode differential amplifiers is their ability to provide a high output resistance, which helps in rejecting common-mode signals. This is due to the fact that the cascode configuration effectively increases the output resistance of the differential pair. Additionally, the use of two transistors in series allows for better matching between the two input transistors, leading to a more balanced design and further improving CMRR.



### Subsection: 8.5b Design and Analysis of Cascode Differential Amplifiers



To design a cascode differential amplifier, the first step is to select the type of transistors to be used in the cascode configuration. This can include bipolar junction transistors (BJTs) or metal-oxide-semiconductor field-effect transistors (MOSFETs). The choice of transistors will depend on the desired gain and other performance requirements.



Once the transistors are selected, the next step is to analyze the circuit using small signal analysis techniques. This involves determining the small signal parameters, such as the transconductance and output resistance, of each transistor in the cascode configuration. These parameters will then be used to calculate the overall gain and other performance metrics of the cascode differential amplifier.



One important consideration in the design of cascode differential amplifiers is the biasing of the transistors. The biasing scheme should ensure that the transistors are operating in their active region and that the cascode configuration is providing the desired output resistance. This can be achieved by using a current mirror or a voltage divider circuit.



Another factor to consider is the stability of the cascode differential amplifier. The cascode configuration can introduce additional poles and zeros in the transfer function, which can affect the stability of the amplifier. Therefore, it is important to carefully choose the values of the components in the circuit to ensure stability.



In terms of performance, cascode differential amplifiers have a higher gain compared to other types of differential amplifiers. This is due to the increased output resistance and improved matching between the input transistors. Additionally, the cascode configuration also helps in reducing distortion and improving linearity.



In conclusion, cascode differential amplifiers are a powerful tool in the design of high-performance differential amplifiers. They offer a higher gain, improved common-mode rejection, and better linearity compared to other types of differential amplifiers. However, careful consideration must be given to the design and biasing of the circuit to ensure stability and optimal performance.

Cascode differential amplifiers are also used in communication systems, such as in radio frequency (RF) receivers, to amplify weak signals and improve the signal-to-noise ratio.



In conclusion, cascode differential amplifiers offer significant advantages over other types of differential amplifiers, making them a popular choice in various applications. Their high gain, high output resistance, and improved linearity make them a valuable tool in the design of microelectronic devices and circuits. 





# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 8: Differential Amplifiers



### Section: 8.6 Symmetrical Differential Amplifiers



In the previous section, we discussed the design and analysis of cascode differential amplifiers. Now, we will explore another type of differential amplifier - the symmetrical differential amplifier.



#### 8.6a Introduction to Symmetrical Differential Amplifiers



Symmetrical differential amplifiers are a type of differential amplifier that use a symmetrical feedback network to eliminate common-mode gain and common-mode bias. This configuration allows for a more balanced design and improved performance compared to other types of differential amplifiers.



One of the main advantages of symmetrical differential amplifiers is their ability to provide a high common-mode rejection ratio (CMRR). This is due to the fact that the symmetrical feedback network effectively cancels out any common-mode input signals, resulting in a CMRR of zero. Additionally, the use of a symmetrical feedback network also helps to eliminate any common-mode bias, leading to a more balanced design.



### Subsection: 8.6b Design and Analysis of Symmetrical Differential Amplifiers



To design a symmetrical differential amplifier, the first step is to select the type of feedback network to be used. This can include a resistor network or a current mirror network. The choice of network will depend on the desired performance requirements.



Once the feedback network is selected, the next step is to analyze the circuit using small signal analysis techniques. This involves determining the small signal parameters, such as the transconductance and output resistance, of each transistor in the differential pair. These parameters will then be used to calculate the overall gain and other performance metrics of the symmetrical differential amplifier.



One of the key advantages of symmetrical differential amplifiers is their ability to eliminate the effects of input bias current and differential input impedance. This is achieved by selecting a feedback network that improves the effect of common-mode input signals and bias. By modeling the input bias current at each terminal and using the Thévenin equivalent for the network driving each terminal, we can simplify the equations and ultimately achieve a closed-loop gain for the differential signal of "V"<sup>+</sup><sub>in</sub> − "V"<sup>−</sup><sub>in</sub>, while the common-mode gain is identically zero.



Furthermore, the use of a symmetrical feedback network also helps to cancel out the common-mode input bias current, leaving only the input offset current "I"<sup>Δ</sup><sub>b</sub> = "I"<sup>+</sup><sub>b</sub> − "I"<sup>−</sup><sub>b</sub> still present. This offset current is equivalent to an input offset voltage acting across an input resistance "R"<sub>i</sub>, which is the source resistance of the feedback network into the input terminals.



In conclusion, symmetrical differential amplifiers offer a balanced and high-performance solution for differential amplification, making them a valuable tool in the field of microelectronics.

Once the feedback network is selected, the next step is to analyze the circuit using small signal analysis techniques. This involves determining the small signal parameters, such as the transconductance and output resistance, of each transistor in the differential pair. These parameters will then be used to calculate the overall gain and other performance metrics of the symmetrical differential amplifier.



One of the key advantages of symmetrical differential amplifiers is their ability to eliminate the effects of input bias current and differential input impedance. This is achieved by selecting a feedback network that improves the effect of common-mode input signal and bias. In Figure 6, current generators model the input bias current at each terminal; "I"<sup>+</sup><sub>b</sub> and "I"<sup>−</sup><sub>b</sub> represent the input bias current at terminals "V"<sup>+</sup> and "V"<sup>−</sup> respectively.



The Thévenin equivalent for the network driving the "V"<sup>+</sup> terminal has a voltage "V"<sup>+</sup>' and impedance "R"<sup>+</sup>':

while for the network driving the "V"<sup>−</sup> terminal:

The output of the op-amp is just the open-loop gain "A"<sub>ol</sub> times the differential input current "i" times the differential input impedance 2"R"<sub>d</sub>, therefore

where "R"<sub>||</sub> is the average of "R"<sup>+</sup><sub>||</sub> and "R"<sup>−</sup><sub>||</sub>.



These equations undergo a great simplification if 

resulting in the relation

which implies that the closed-loop gain for the differential signal is "V"<sup>+</sup><sub>in</sub> − "V"<sup>−</sup><sub>in</sub>, but the common-mode gain is identically zero. 



It also implies that the common-mode input bias current has cancelled out, leaving only the input offset current "I"<sup>Δ</sup><sub>b</sub> = "I"<sup>+</sup><sub>b</sub> − "I"<sup>−</sup><sub>b</sub> still present, and with a coefficient of "R"<sub>i</sub>. It is as if the input offset current is equivalent to an input offset voltage acting across an input resistance "R"<sub>i</sub>, which is the source resistance of the feedback network into the input terminals.



Finally, as long as the open-loop voltage gain "A"<sub>ol</sub> is much larger than unity, the closed-loop voltage gain is "R"<sub>f</sub>/"R"<sub>i</sub>, the value one would obtain through the rule-of-thumb analysis known as the "golden rule". This makes symmetrical differential amplifiers a popular choice for high-performance applications where a high CMRR and balanced design are crucial.

## Chapter: Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



### Introduction



In this chapter, we will delve into the world of multi-stage amplifiers, an essential component in modern electronic devices. Amplifiers are electronic circuits that are used to increase the amplitude of a signal, making them crucial in a wide range of applications such as audio systems, communication systems, and medical devices. Multi-stage amplifiers, as the name suggests, are amplifiers that consist of multiple stages or individual amplifying units connected in a series. These amplifiers are designed to provide high gain and low distortion, making them ideal for use in high-performance electronic systems.



The chapter will begin with an overview of the basic principles of amplifiers and their classification. We will then move on to discuss the different types of multi-stage amplifiers, including cascade, cascode, and Darlington amplifiers. Each type will be explained in detail, highlighting their advantages and disadvantages. We will also cover the design considerations for multi-stage amplifiers, including biasing, frequency response, and stability.



Next, we will explore the concept of gain and its importance in multi-stage amplifiers. We will discuss the different types of gain, such as voltage gain, current gain, and power gain, and how they are calculated. We will also touch upon the concept of feedback and its role in determining the overall gain of a multi-stage amplifier.



The chapter will then move on to discuss the analysis and design of multi-stage amplifiers using the popular small-signal model. We will cover the different stages of analysis, including DC analysis, AC analysis, and frequency response analysis. We will also provide step-by-step examples to illustrate the design process of multi-stage amplifiers.



Finally, we will conclude the chapter with a discussion on the limitations and challenges of multi-stage amplifiers. We will explore the various factors that can affect the performance of these amplifiers, such as noise, distortion, and temperature. We will also touch upon the advancements in technology that have led to the development of more efficient and reliable multi-stage amplifiers.



In summary, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to multi-stage amplifiers, covering their principles, types, design considerations, and limitations. With the knowledge gained from this chapter, readers will be able to understand the functioning of multi-stage amplifiers and apply them in their own electronic designs. 





# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 9: Multi-stage Amplifiers



### Section 9.1: Cascading Differential Stages



In this section, we will explore the concept of cascading differential stages in multi-stage amplifiers. Differential stages are a type of amplifier that uses two transistors to amplify a differential input signal. Cascading refers to the connection of multiple stages in a series, where the output of one stage is connected to the input of the next stage.



#### 9.1a: Introduction to Cascading Differential Stages



Cascading differential stages are a common technique used in multi-stage amplifiers to achieve high gain and low distortion. This is because each stage in the cascade provides additional amplification, resulting in a higher overall gain. Additionally, the use of differential stages helps to reduce distortion by canceling out common-mode signals.



The process of cascading differential stages can be understood by considering the two-integrator backstepping method. In this method, a multiple-integrator system is built up from subsystems of already-stabilized multiple-integrator subsystems. Similarly, in cascading differential stages, each stage is designed to be stable and provide amplification, and then these stages are connected in a series to achieve a higher overall gain.



The stability of each stage is crucial in cascading differential stages. If one stage is unstable, it can affect the stability of the entire cascade. Therefore, careful design considerations must be taken into account to ensure the stability of each stage.



In the next subsection, we will discuss the different types of cascading differential stages, including cascade, cascode, and Darlington amplifiers, and their advantages and disadvantages. 





# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 9: Multi-stage Amplifiers



### Section 9.1: Cascading Differential Stages



In this section, we will explore the concept of cascading differential stages in multi-stage amplifiers. Differential stages are a type of amplifier that uses two transistors to amplify a differential input signal. Cascading refers to the connection of multiple stages in a series, where the output of one stage is connected to the input of the next stage.



#### 9.1a: Introduction to Cascading Differential Stages



Cascading differential stages are a common technique used in multi-stage amplifiers to achieve high gain and low distortion. This is because each stage in the cascade provides additional amplification, resulting in a higher overall gain. Additionally, the use of differential stages helps to reduce distortion by canceling out common-mode signals.



The process of cascading differential stages can be understood by considering the two-integrator backstepping method. In this method, a multiple-integrator system is built up from subsystems of already-stabilized multiple-integrator subsystems. Similarly, in cascading differential stages, each stage is designed to be stable and provide amplification, and then these stages are connected in a series to achieve a higher overall gain.



The stability of each stage is crucial in cascading differential stages. If one stage is unstable, it can affect the stability of the entire cascade. Therefore, careful design considerations must be taken into account to ensure the stability of each stage.



In the next subsection, we will discuss the different types of cascading differential stages, including cascade, cascode, and Darlington amplifiers, and their advantages and disadvantages. 



#### 9.1b: Cascading Differential Stages in Multi-stage Amplifiers



In multi-stage amplifiers, cascading differential stages are used to achieve high gain and low distortion. As mentioned in the previous subsection, each stage in the cascade provides additional amplification, resulting in a higher overall gain. This is especially important in applications where high gain is necessary, such as in switched capacitor filters, integrators, and analog-to-digital converters.



One way to implement a two-stage amplifier is by using a folded cascode (FC) amplifier as the first stage and a common source amplifier as the second stage. This combination allows for high gain and high output swing, which is particularly important in modern CMOS technologies where transistors have smaller output resistance due to shorter channel length.



For even higher gain, four-stage amplifiers can be implemented by cascading three common source amplifiers with an FC amplifier. However, as the number of stages increases, the stability of the amplifier becomes a concern. Unlike single-stage amplifiers, multi-stage amplifiers have multiple poles and can become unstable when used in feedback networks. To ensure stability, a compensation network is necessary.



The main goal of the compensation network is to modify the transfer function of the system to achieve enough phase margin (PM). This is typically done by using a compensation capacitor to move the dominant pole to a lower frequency, similar to how a compensation capacitor is used in single-stage amplifiers. However, in multi-stage amplifiers, the compensation capacitor is used to compensate for the multiple poles in the system.



In conclusion, cascading differential stages in multi-stage amplifiers allow for high gain and low distortion, making them essential in various applications. However, careful consideration must be taken to ensure the stability of each stage and the overall amplifier.

## Chapter 9: Multi-stage Amplifiers



### Section 9.1: Cascading Differential Stages



In this section, we will explore the concept of cascading differential stages in multi-stage amplifiers. Differential stages are a type of amplifier that uses two transistors to amplify a differential input signal. Cascading refers to the connection of multiple stages in a series, where the output of one stage is connected to the input of the next stage.



#### 9.1a: Introduction to Cascading Differential Stages



Cascading differential stages are a common technique used in multi-stage amplifiers to achieve high gain and low distortion. This is because each stage in the cascade provides additional amplification, resulting in a higher overall gain. Additionally, the use of differential stages helps to reduce distortion by canceling out common-mode signals.



The process of cascading differential stages can be understood by considering the two-integrator backstepping method. In this method, a multiple-integrator system is built up from subsystems of already-stabilized multiple-integrator subsystems. Similarly, in cascading differential stages, each stage is designed to be stable and provide amplification, and then these stages are connected in a series to achieve a higher overall gain.



The stability of each stage is crucial in cascading differential stages. If one stage is unstable, it can affect the stability of the entire cascade. Therefore, careful design considerations must be taken into account to ensure the stability of each stage.



In the next subsection, we will discuss the different types of cascading differential stages, including cascade, cascode, and Darlington amplifiers, and their advantages and disadvantages. 



#### 9.1b: Cascading Differential Stages in Multi-stage Amplifiers



In multi-stage amplifiers, cascading differential stages are used to achieve high gain and low distortion. As mentioned in the previous section, each stage in the cascade provides additional amplification, resulting in a higher overall gain. This is especially useful in applications where a high gain is required, such as in audio amplifiers or communication systems.



One type of cascading differential stage is the cascade amplifier, where the output of one stage is connected to the input of the next stage. This allows for a higher overall gain, as the output of each stage is amplified by the next stage. However, this type of amplifier can also introduce more noise and distortion, as any noise or distortion from one stage is amplified by the subsequent stages.



Another type is the cascode amplifier, where the output of one stage is connected to the input of the next stage, but with a common-source or common-emitter configuration. This helps to reduce noise and distortion, as the common-mode signals are canceled out. However, this type of amplifier has a lower overall gain compared to the cascade amplifier.



Lastly, the Darlington amplifier is a type of cascading differential stage where two transistors are connected in a common-emitter configuration. This allows for a very high gain, but at the cost of increased complexity and potential stability issues.



In the next subsection, we will discuss the applications of cascading differential stages in more detail.



#### 9.1c: Applications of Cascading Differential Stages



Cascading differential stages have a wide range of applications in microelectronic devices and circuits. One of the most common applications is in audio amplifiers, where a high gain and low distortion are essential for high-quality sound reproduction.



In communication systems, cascading differential stages are used to amplify weak signals received from antennas or sensors. The high gain provided by cascading stages allows for better signal detection and processing.



Cascading differential stages are also used in instrumentation amplifiers, which are used to amplify small signals from sensors or transducers. The high gain and low distortion provided by cascading stages are crucial in accurately measuring and amplifying these small signals.



In conclusion, cascading differential stages are a powerful tool in the design of multi-stage amplifiers. They provide high gain and low distortion, making them essential in various applications such as audio amplifiers, communication systems, and instrumentation amplifiers. However, careful consideration must be taken in the design and stability of each stage to ensure the overall performance of the cascade. 





# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 9: Multi-stage Amplifiers



### Section 9.2: Output Stages



In the previous section, we discussed the concept of cascading differential stages in multi-stage amplifiers. Now, we will focus on the output stages of these amplifiers. The output stage is responsible for delivering the amplified signal to the load while maintaining the desired gain and minimizing distortion.



#### 9.2a: Introduction to Output Stages



The output stage is the final stage in a multi-stage amplifier and is responsible for delivering the amplified signal to the load. It is designed to have a high output impedance to minimize the loading effect on the previous stages. The output stage also needs to have a high gain to compensate for any losses in the previous stages.



In addition to these requirements, the output stage must also have a low output impedance to drive the load effectively. This is achieved by using a push-pull configuration, where two transistors are used to drive the load in opposite directions. This helps to reduce distortion and improve the linearity of the output signal.



In the next subsection, we will discuss the different types of output stages, including class A, class B, and class AB, and their advantages and disadvantages.



#### 9.2b: Types of Output Stages



There are three main types of output stages used in multi-stage amplifiers: class A, class B, and class AB. Each type has its own advantages and disadvantages, and the choice of output stage depends on the specific requirements of the amplifier.



##### Class A Output Stage



The class A output stage is the simplest type of output stage and is commonly used in low-power amplifiers. In this configuration, the transistor is biased to operate in the active region, where it conducts current throughout the entire input signal cycle. This results in a high output impedance and a low distortion output signal.



However, the main disadvantage of the class A output stage is its low efficiency. As the transistor is always conducting current, it dissipates a significant amount of power, leading to high power consumption and heat generation.



##### Class B Output Stage



The class B output stage is more efficient than the class A output stage as it uses two transistors, one for each half of the input signal cycle. This allows each transistor to operate in the active region for only half of the cycle, reducing power dissipation and increasing efficiency.



However, the main disadvantage of the class B output stage is crossover distortion, where there is a discontinuity in the output signal at the point where one transistor switches off and the other switches on. This can be mitigated by using a class AB output stage.



##### Class AB Output Stage



The class AB output stage combines the advantages of both class A and class B output stages. It uses two transistors like the class B stage, but the transistors are biased to operate in the active region for a small portion of the input signal cycle, reducing crossover distortion.



The class AB output stage is more efficient than the class A stage but less efficient than the class B stage. It also has a lower distortion than the class B stage but higher distortion than the class A stage. Therefore, it is a good compromise between efficiency and distortion.



In the next section, we will discuss the design considerations for output stages and how to choose the appropriate output stage for a given amplifier.





# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 9: Multi-stage Amplifiers



### Section 9.2: Output Stages



In the previous section, we discussed the concept of cascading differential stages in multi-stage amplifiers. Now, we will focus on the output stages of these amplifiers. The output stage is responsible for delivering the amplified signal to the load while maintaining the desired gain and minimizing distortion.



#### 9.2a: Introduction to Output Stages



The output stage is the final stage in a multi-stage amplifier and is responsible for delivering the amplified signal to the load. It is designed to have a high output impedance to minimize the loading effect on the previous stages. The output stage also needs to have a high gain to compensate for any losses in the previous stages.



In addition to these requirements, the output stage must also have a low output impedance to drive the load effectively. This is achieved by using a push-pull configuration, where two transistors are used to drive the load in opposite directions. This helps to reduce distortion and improve the linearity of the output signal.



In the next subsection, we will discuss the different types of output stages, including class A, class B, and class AB, and their advantages and disadvantages.



#### 9.2b: Types of Output Stages



There are three main types of output stages used in multi-stage amplifiers: class A, class B, and class AB. Each type has its own advantages and disadvantages, and the choice of output stage depends on the specific requirements of the amplifier.



##### Class A Output Stage



The class A output stage is the simplest type of output stage and is commonly used in low-power amplifiers. In this configuration, the transistor is biased to operate in the active region, where it conducts current throughout the entire input signal cycle. This results in a high output impedance and a low distortion output signal.



However, the main disadvantage of the class A output stage is its low efficiency, as the transistor is always conducting current even when there is no input signal. This leads to high power dissipation and limits its use in high-power applications.



##### Class B Output Stage



The class B output stage overcomes the low efficiency of the class A stage by using two transistors, one for the positive half of the input signal and one for the negative half. This allows each transistor to operate in the active region for only half of the input signal cycle, reducing power dissipation.



However, the main disadvantage of the class B output stage is the presence of crossover distortion, where there is a small gap between the positive and negative halves of the output signal. This can be mitigated by using a class AB output stage.



##### Class AB Output Stage



The class AB output stage combines the advantages of both class A and class B stages. It uses two transistors like the class B stage, but they are biased to operate in the active region for a small portion of the input signal cycle, reducing crossover distortion.



This results in a higher efficiency compared to the class A stage, while also reducing distortion compared to the class B stage. However, the design and biasing of the class AB output stage can be more complex and may require additional compensation networks to achieve stability.



In the next subsection, we will discuss the stability of multi-stage amplifiers and the use of compensation networks.



#### 9.2c: Stability in Multi-stage Amplifiers



Unlike single-stage amplifiers, multi-stage amplifiers usually have three or more poles, and if they are used in feedback networks, the closed-loop system is likely to be unstable. To ensure stable behavior, it is necessary to use a compensation network.



The main goal of a compensation network is to modify the transfer function of the system to achieve enough phase margin (PM). This is similar to the use of a compensation capacitor in single-stage amplifiers, where it helps to improve PM by moving the dominant pole to a lower frequency.



In multi-stage amplifiers, a compensation capacitor is usually used to move the dominant pole to a lower frequency, ensuring enough PM for stability. The specific design and placement of the compensation network will depend on the specific amplifier configuration and requirements.



In the next section, we will discuss the design and analysis of multi-stage amplifiers in more detail.

# Microelectronic Devices and Circuits: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 9: Multi-stage Amplifiers



### Section 9.3: Active Loads



In the previous section, we discussed the different types of output stages used in multi-stage amplifiers. Now, we will focus on the active loads used in these stages. An active load, also known as a dynamic load, is a component or circuit that functions as a current-stable nonlinear resistor. It is designed to provide a high small-signal impedance without requiring a large DC voltage drop.



#### 9.3a: Introduction to Active Loads



Active loads are an essential component in multi-stage amplifiers as they help to increase the AC gain and improve the linearity of the output signal. They are typically used in the output stage of the amplifier, where a high output impedance is required to minimize the loading effect on the previous stages.



In the next subsection, we will discuss the different types of active loads commonly used in multi-stage amplifiers.



#### 9.3b: Types of Active Loads



There are several types of active loads used in multi-stage amplifiers, including the constant-current resistor, the current mirror, and the cascode circuit. Each type has its own advantages and disadvantages, and the choice of active load depends on the specific requirements of the amplifier.



##### Constant-Current Resistor



The constant-current resistor is the simplest type of active load and is commonly used in low-power amplifiers. It is made up of a transistor biased to operate in the active region, where it acts as a current source. This results in a high output impedance and a low distortion output signal.



However, the main disadvantage of this type of active load is that it requires a large DC voltage drop, which can limit the output signal swing and reduce the overall efficiency of the amplifier.



##### Current Mirror



The current mirror is a more complex type of active load that is commonly used in high-power amplifiers. It is made up of two transistors, where one transistor is biased to operate in the active region, while the other transistor is used as a load. This results in a high output impedance and a low distortion output signal.



The advantage of using a current mirror as an active load is that it does not require a large DC voltage drop, making it more efficient than the constant-current resistor. However, it is more complex and requires precise matching of the two transistors to function effectively.



##### Cascode Circuit



The cascode circuit is a combination of the constant-current resistor and the current mirror. It is commonly used in high-frequency amplifiers as it provides a high output impedance and a low distortion output signal. It also has the advantage of not requiring a large DC voltage drop.



However, the cascode circuit is more complex and requires precise matching of the transistors, making it more expensive to implement.



In the next section, we will discuss the design considerations for active loads in multi-stage amplifiers.

## Chapter 9: Multi-stage Amplifiers



### Section 9.3: Active Loads



In the previous section, we discussed the different types of output stages used in multi-stage amplifiers. Now, we will focus on the active loads used in these stages. An active load, also known as a dynamic load, is a component or circuit that functions as a current-stable nonlinear resistor. It is designed to provide a high small-signal impedance without requiring a large DC voltage drop.



#### 9.3a: Introduction to Active Loads



Active loads are an essential component in multi-stage amplifiers as they help to increase the AC gain and improve the linearity of the output signal. They are typically used in the output stage of the amplifier, where a high output impedance is required to minimize the loading effect on the previous stages.



In the next subsection, we will discuss the different types of active loads commonly used in multi-stage amplifiers.



#### 9.3b: Types of Active Loads



There are several types of active loads used in multi-stage amplifiers, including the constant-current resistor, the current mirror, and the cascode circuit. Each type has its own advantages and disadvantages, and the choice of active load depends on the specific requirements of the amplifier.



##### Constant-Current Resistor



The constant-current resistor is the simplest type of active load and is commonly used in low-power amplifiers. It is made up of a transistor biased to operate in the active region, where it acts as a current source. This results in a high output impedance and a low distortion output signal.



However, the main disadvantage of this type of active load is that it requires a large DC voltage drop, which can limit the output signal swing and reduce the overall efficiency of the amplifier.



##### Current Mirror



The current mirror is a more complex type of active load that is commonly used in high-power amplifiers. It is made up of two transistors, one acting as a reference and the other as a load. The reference transistor is biased to operate in the active region, while the load transistor is biased to operate in the saturation region. This results in a high output impedance and a low distortion output signal.



One advantage of the current mirror is that it does not require a large DC voltage drop, making it more efficient than the constant-current resistor. However, it is more complex and requires precise matching of the two transistors.



##### Cascode Circuit



The cascode circuit is another type of active load that is commonly used in high-frequency amplifiers. It is made up of two transistors, one acting as a common-emitter amplifier and the other as a common-base amplifier. This results in a high output impedance and a low distortion output signal.



One advantage of the cascode circuit is that it provides a high gain and a wide bandwidth. However, it is more complex and requires precise matching of the two transistors.



In the next subsection, we will discuss the design considerations for active loads in multi-stage amplifiers.

### Section 9.3: Active Loads



In the previous section, we discussed the different types of output stages used in multi-stage amplifiers. Now, we will focus on the active loads used in these stages. It is designed to provide a high small-signal impedance without requiring a large DC voltage drop.



#### 9.3a: Introduction to Active Loads



Active loads are an essential component in multi-stage amplifiers as they help to increase the AC gain and improve the linearity of the output signal. They are typically used in the output stage of the amplifier, where a high output impedance is required to minimize the loading effect on the previous stages.



In the next subsection, we will discuss the different types of active loads commonly used in multi-stage amplifiers.



#### 9.3b: Types of Active Loads



There are several types of active loads used in multi-stage amplifiers, including the constant-current resistor, the current mirror, and the cascode circuit. Each type has its own advantages and disadvantages, and the choice of active load depends on the specific requirements of the amplifier.



##### Constant-Current Resistor



The constant-current resistor is the simplest type of active load and is commonly used in low-power amplifiers. It is made up of a transistor biased to operate in the active region, where it acts as a current source. This results in a high output impedance and a low distortion output signal.



However, the main disadvantage of this type of active load is that it requires a large DC voltage drop, which can limit the output signal swing and reduce the overall efficiency of the amplifier.



##### Current Mirror



The current mirror is a more complex type of active load that is commonly used in high-power amplifiers. It is made up of two transistors, one acting as a reference and the other as a load. The reference transistor is biased to operate in the active region, while the load transistor is biased to operate in the saturation region. This results in a high output impedance and a low distortion output signal.

##### Cascode Circuit



The cascode circuit is a combination of the constant-current resistor and the current mirror. It is commonly used in high-frequency amplifiers as it provides a high output impedance and low distortion output signal. It is made up of two transistors, one acting as a current source and the other as a load. The load transistor is connected in a cascode configuration with the output transistor, resulting in a high output impedance.



One advantage of the cascode circuit is that it provides a high output impedance without requiring a large DC voltage drop. However, it is more complex and requires precise matching of the two transistors, similar to the current mirror.



In the next subsection, we will discuss the applications of active loads in multi-stage amplifiers.



#### 9.3c: Applications of Active Loads



Active loads have various applications in multi-stage amplifiers, including increasing the AC gain, improving linearity, and minimizing the loading effect on previous stages. They are commonly used in the output stage of the amplifier, but can also be used in other stages depending on the specific requirements of the circuit.



One application of active loads is in high-frequency amplifiers, where a high output impedance is necessary to minimize the loading effect on the previous stages. The cascode circuit is commonly used in these amplifiers due to its high output impedance and low distortion output signal.



Another application is in low-power amplifiers, where a high output impedance is necessary to improve the linearity of the output signal. The constant-current resistor is commonly used in these amplifiers, despite its disadvantage of requiring a large DC voltage drop.



In summary, active loads are an essential component in multi-stage amplifiers, providing a high output impedance and improving the linearity of the output signal. The choice of active load depends on the specific requirements of the amplifier, and different types can be used in different stages to achieve the desired performance.
